tions. Four elegant prizes will be given
at the ball. The music for the occasion
will be furnished by the Alcazar orches- |
Orand march at 9 o'clock. The ball
will be the social event of the year.

SENATOR SAILS
WITH TROOPS
FOR MANILA
The Valencia to Leave
To- Morrow.
THEY ARE TO 60 BY RANK
GEN. MILLER WILL REMAIN
HERE TILL THE LAST.
He Will Finally Sail on the New
port, Accompanied by a Bat
talion of tho Tennessee
Regiment.
It was about 9:30 o'clock yesterday
morning when the 800 troops designated
for the Senator marched from the Pre
sidio. As has been stated in The Call,
they were the Third Battalion of the
Twenty-third Infantry, the recruits of the
Second Oregon Regiment and a detach
ment from the California Heavy Artillery.
The line of march was down Lombard
street and thence by the water front to
Pacific-street wharf, where the Sen
ator lay, and this accounted Cor the tus
appointment of those who had gathered
along the streets to witness the march
ing away of the soldiers. The troops were
all aboard by noon and about 3 o'clock
the Senator cast off her moorings and
slipped out Into the stream, and then as
she started on her long journey to Ma
nila she was saluted by a blast from the
whistles of all the tugs and steamers in
the harbor. Major Goodale of the Twen
ty-third Infantry was In command of tae
Senator.
Orders were issued from department
headquarters late yesterday afternoon by
General Merriam changing the pro
gramme as mapped out for the sailing of
the Valencia. The troops were to have
gone aboard of this transport to-day, and
she was to have sailed this evening. This
was the prevailing order up to 5 o'clock
yesterday afternoon, and in compliance
with it the California Heavy Artillery nad
got their heavy baggage aboard, when Col
onel Long was imormed that the Valen
cia could not land at Angel Island for the
Second Battalion of the first "Washington.
This necessitated a change. The artil
lery was informed that it would not go on
board until to-morrow morning instead of
to-day. This delays the sailing of the
transport one day. To-day the McDowell
wul bring over the heavy baggage of tae
Washington troops and to-morrow vie
battalion will be brought over. Just as
soon as it is got aboard the \ alencia
will sail. The remainder of the Washing
ton regiment and all, except ninety men
of the California Heavy Artillery, will
get away on the Ohio, which may *>os
sibly sail this week.
One important thing has been deter
mined in regard to the sailing of the three
regiments which remain. Tne regiments
ar?' to go according to the seniority of
their colonels. This will take the Twen
tieth Kansas first, the Flfty-Hrst lowa
second and the Tennessee last. A bat
talion of this regiment will go on the
Newport with General Miller, who is to
remain until all of the expeditionary
forces have been got away. Now tuat
the transports are nearly all here, General
Merriam does not think that It will be
very long before the last of the troops
will have gone. He stated yesterday that
they would be sent away just as fast a*
the vessels were ready for them. A
month at the very outside will be suffi
cient time to get all the forces off.
For the benefit of those who wish to
forward packages to their friends among
the soldiers at Manila, the department
commander gives out the announcement
that all packages not exceeding 12 by 18 by
10 inches will bo received up to the 3ist
inst. They must be delivered to the depot
quartermaster, all charges having been
paid, and will then be forwarded on the
iirst transport to the islands. Perishable
goods will not be received.

TRIED TO WRECK A SALOON.
Warrants Out for the Arrest of a
Vicious Gang in the Mission.
Warrants were issued yesterday by
Judge Conlan for the arrest of William
Donellan, Frank McQuade, George
Boyle, William Burns and two others on
a charge of disturbing the peace. The
complaining witness is Otto Methmann,
proprietor of a saloon at Seventeenth and
Noe streets.
The defendants are young men and
boys who. the police say, terrorize the
neighborhood. Saturday night they en
tered Methmann's saloon and attempted
to clean out the place. They took away
part of his stove and the bread from the
counter. Donellan and Boyle were ar
rested early Saturday morning and
charged with malicious mischief. The
cases were called in Judge Conlan's court
yesterday and continued. As Donellan
failed to appear his bail of $20 was de
clared forfeited and a bench warrant was
issued for his arrest.
The gang is also accused of tearing
down election signs and pulling up pole 3
for holding election banners.
MAYOR PHELAN SAID
"HELLO" TO PORTLAND
TELEPHONE WIRES STI/TJNG TO
THE FAR NORTH.
An Official Conversation Between the
Executive Officers of Both
Cities.
"Hello, Portland. Hello, hello. What?
Get closer to the phone. What? All
right. Get the jwayor there."
"Hello. Mayor Phelan. Here's Portland.
Talk to them."
This brief conversation started the talk
between the Mayors of the two great
cities of the Pacific Coast yesterday after
noon, after the telephone company got all
the "bugs" off the wires. It is an accom
plished fact, and one can now talk to
Portland or Seattle Just as easy as to
Oakland. This was proven conclusively
when the Mayors of the two cities talked
and extended congratulations yesterday.
Mayor Phelan was called to his telephone,
and being prepared for the ordeal imme
diately began his address, interlarding his
remarks with frequent inquiries as to
whether he was heard or not. The re
sponses being satisfactory, he said:
"San Francisco is much gratified to
have been brought within direct speaking
distance of the city of Portland. Such
service will tend to draw us closer to
gether and unify the interests of the Pa
cific Coast."
To this Mayor W. S. Mason of Portland
responded:
"May the commercial relations be
woven more closely since the means of
communication are by this method a
great advance over all previous facilities.
May the cities of San Francisco and Port
land ever occupy the position of leaders
in all enterprises that will advance the
interests of the Pacific Coast."
A brief good-by ended the conversation
and the little ceremony was over. Aufll
ble communication between the two cities
more than 600 miles apart was established,
and henceforth if you want to talk it is
simply a question of having the price.
After the close of the official conversa
tion several invited guests at both ends
of the line held short talks, and then the
party at this end of the line went over
the big building and examined the vari
ous departments from roof to lower floor.
The telephone company has been work
ing Industriously for several months in
Its efforts to get the long distance wires
to the far north, and now congratulates
itself upon the accomplishment of the
fact. The heavy copper wires have been
strung until a network covers the north
western territory, and as a result the
commercial interests of the entire Pacific
Coast will be greatly improved and aided.
It is expected by the company that
aluminumJwires will replace those of cop
per at an early date, and as the former
are lighter than copper It will cause not
only a great saving in the actual cost of
the wires, but will materially reduce the
number of poles on which the wires are
strung.
A LOVELORN RANCHER.
Andy Anzene of Fort Bragg Is De
termined to Wed Nellie Buckley.
Andy Anzene, the rnnrher from Fort
Bragg, who was arrested last Thursday
on a charge of burglary for stealing a
silk dress from the apartments of Miss
Nellie Buckley, 118 Sixth street, had the
case dismissed yesterday by Judge Mogan
for lack of prosecution. Miss Buckley
appeared in court and said she did not
want to prosecute, as Anzeno had re
turned the dress. In fact she did not
want to have anything more to do with
him.
Andy told the Judge that he proposed
to start a butcher shop on Sixth street,
and he would still continue to court Nel
lie, as he was determined to make her
his wife. He had loved her at Fort Bragg
and he loved her still.
A Pugilistic Baker.
Henry Bender, a baker, was arrested
yesterday afternoon on a warrant charg
ing him with battery. The complaining
witness is Joseph Ott janitor in the Bak
ers' Home, on Mission and Fourth
streets. Saturday night Bender took of
fense at something Ott had done and
gave him an unmerciful thrashing. Hia
race was badly cut and his eyes black
ened and swollen.
BONDSMEN MAY
HAVE TO PAY
SCHOOL BILLS
Auditor Broderick Is
Alarmed.
WARNS THE SCHOOL BOARD
EXCESS OF EXPENSES OVER
INCOME CHARGED.
Debt Cannot Be Carried Over and
Creditors Have No Recourse
Except Against the
Sureties.
Auditor Broderick vlsfled Secretary
Welsh of the School Board yesterday aft
ernoon and served formal warning
through him to the members of the board
that they were In danger of grave compli
cations affecting their bondsmen. The
Auditor said: "If the present rate of ex
penditures by the School Board is main
tained I do not see anything ahead ex
cept suits against the bondsmen of tho
members by the claimants on unpaid bills
contracted by the board for which them
is no money to pay. The law expressly
forbids the Incurring of such debts above
the limit of appropriations. When the
thing is done the bondsmen are legally
liable. Last evening one of the members
"nf the School Board stated to me that
tnÂ£ board had already incurred indebted
ness to the extent of $70,000 in excess* of
its revenues. If this be true then I think
that the bondsmen In question will hayÂ«
the privilege of paying for certain new
schoolhouses and lots, Instead of the tax-
payers.
"The board has a fixed liability for sal
aries and can easily see whether it is
running ahead of its income. When it
reaches the limit of its funds this year
the Auditor cannot allow any more claima
and there Is no way of carrying the debt
over into next year, as the law only al
lows the School Board a certain sum per
capita, and that sum has been secured
this yoar to the last dollar. It would bo
dodging the statutes to attempt to collect
money next year to pay the overexpendl
tures of this year, and the courts have al
ready held that such an evasion of the in
tent of the law cannot be tolerated. It
would be well for the members of tho
Board of Education to fully understand
this matter now, before they get into the
mire hopelessly. As things stand now,
they are in a bad box, provided their
fund is overexpended, as I have reason to
believe from thfi statement made to me."
Secretary Welsh protested that the
board could not know what Its full appro
priation would be until the close of the
fiscal year, as one-half of the State money
comes in at that time. He thought that
the appropriation would be considerably
larger from the State this year than It
was last year.
At the last meeting of the board Direct
or Head presented a statement that the
board was almost hopelessly involved fin
ancially, and warning it to desist. He
was opposed to the appointment of an
other teacher or the incurring of further
expenses of any kind.
Auditor Broderick's statement will fall
like a very wet blanket upon the Board
of Education, and will probably result In
pome careful financial calculations within
the next few days.
St. John's Bazaar Entertainment
St. John's Bazaar, which is being held
in the old St. Mary's College building, is
enjoying the greatest success. Every
evening a large crowd is in attendance
and small and large sums are spent in an
effort to aid the undertaking. On Wed
nesday evening St. Anthony r s booth will
entertain the visitors with a fine pro
gramme. Mrs. Thomas Minihan and Mrs.
John Mitchell, who preside over the booth,
have prevailed on the following well
known artists to appear: Kelly & Mar
lowe, in their specialties; Miss L. Resing,
vocal solo; Kittie Meda and Miss Hazel
Thornton and Miss Emma Newman, fancy
dances; Miss Madgo Connolly, reading;
Loughery's orchestra will furnish music
during tho evening.
Patriots to Be Elevated.
The United States Civil Service Commis
sion has placed the position of elevator
conductor on the civil service list and will
establish an eligible list from which va
cancies may be filled. Applicants mun
be citizens and between the ages of 21
and 60 years, and are required to show
ability and physical qualifications and to
have had experience. Residents of San
Francisco will have precedence. Persons
desiring to enter the examination should
apply to Miss Belle C. Lewald, secretary
of the Board of Examiners at the Main
D Postofflce on Washington street.
Divorce Suits Filed.
Joseph L. Patnaude has applied for a
divorce from Mamie Patnaude, alleging
Infidelity as a cause of action. Ida Con
stantine asks for a divorce from George
D. Constantine on the ground of deser
tion. Florence M. Collins alleges that her
husband, Frederick R. Collins, has been,
accustomed to treat her in a cruel man
ner, and she asks for a divorce on that
ground.

Cadets Will Entertain.
Company H, League of the Cross Cadets,
will give a grand musical and literature
entertainment (and descriptive war
views), at Metropolitan Temple on Thurs
day evening, November 3. A feature of
the entertainment will be the distribu
tion of medals won by the various con
testants at the field day, held at Glen
Ellen, September 9, together with the
handsome trophy, donated by Mayor
James D. Phelan, and won by this com
pany. The descriptive war views to be
shown are from a rare collection, consist
ing of views from Stntiago de Cuba, Ma
nila and its surroundings.
In all a select programme has been ar
ranged and an enjoyable evening's enter
tainment can be anticipated by all who
attend. The object of this entertainment
is to assist in raising a fund with which
tliis company Intend to erect their own
armory. The committee in charge of the
entertainment is as follows: John Keat
ing chairman: James E. Power, Thomas
Eceles, John Whelan. John Daly, Thomas
Phelan, W. McMahon, W. Mouge, James
Needham, John T. Sullivan and William
Coogan.
PROJECTED NEW FERRY.
Meetings Called for the Purpose of
Promoting the Enterprise.
Interest in the projected new passenger
ferry between Oakland and San Fran
cisco is increasing rapidly, and it Is evi
dent the enterprise will receive substan
tial support from people across the bay.
The property owners of Lorin are con
sidering the proposition to give the
scheme thoir financial support and have
invited the representatives of the Realty
Syndicate, Messrs. Beardalee and Lloyd,
to explain the plans of the company at a
public meeting to be held at that place
this evening.
A committee of the prominent citizens
of Golden Gate have called a meeting of
the realty holders of that section, to oe
herfl to-morrow evening. It is ex
pected that it will be largely attended
and that the gathering will result In a
healthy list of subscriptions to the in
vestment shares of the syndicate.
The meeting for the benefit of the peo
ple of Berkeley will be held some time
next week.
Patriotic Banquet.
The California Society of the Sons of the
American Revolution will commemorate
the surrender of Yorktown by Lord Corn
wallis at a banquet at the California Ho
tel on Wednesday evening. October 19.
During the course of the banquet promi
nent speakers will speak upon the fol
lowing interesting subjects: "American
Imperialism, or Our New Colonial Pol
icy "YeÃterday. To-Day and To-Morrow
in American History," "The American
Navy at Yorktown and Santiago," "The
American Nary From Bern Homme Rich
ard to the Oregon," "An Anglo-Saxon Al
liance." "The American Surgeon Upon the
Modern Battlefield." "America in the Ori
ent " "The Battle of Yalu River, and the
Battle of July 3 Contrasted," and "An
Imperial Democracy."
TOO BUSY TO
ATTEND TO THE
CITY BUSINESS
Supervisors Did Not
Have a Quorum.
FIRST FAILURE IN YEARS
FIGHT OVER THE GAS VETO IS
POSTPONED.
The Mutual Company Serves Notice
of Its Intention to Contest the
Lighting Contract in
the Courts.
The Board of Supervisors did not meet
yesterday afternoon. It was the first time
in four years that a Monday came and
went without a session of the board.
Perhaps the most surprised persons in the
Supervisors' chambers were the six city
fathers who had responded to the usual
call for a meeting and discovered that
there would be none.
"Where's Dodge? Where's that mili
tary Supervisor?" These and similar
questions were propounded In vain.
Dodge, Rottanzl, Britt, Smith and De
lany could not be found. Some suspicious
person among vie onlookers started the
report that the absentees had put up a
job to stay away until Supervisor Morton
returned, so that they could muster the
necessary nine votes to pass a resolution
over the Mayor's veto to pay to the San
Francisco Gas and Electric Company the
disputed bills for August gas amounting
to $8696 50 and the September account,
aggregating $15,928 55 for street lighting.
This story lacked strength, however, as
Dodge was known to be opposed to the
payment of the bills in question. Later
he effectually disposed of the allegation,
so far as he was concerned, by appearing
at the Supervisors' rooms. He had been
out electioneering and was, he said, de
pending upon the dilatory ways of the
Supervisors to get into the meeting before
it closed. The other absentees did not
show up and their excuses will have to be
filed later. Suffice it to say that Mayor
Phelan adjourned the board to meet at
his call, probably some time before next
Monday.
The attorneys for the Mutual Electric
Company, Messrs. Lloyd & Wood, had
given written notice to Auditor Broderick
during the afternoon that they would
serve injunction papers on him immedi
ately, in case the Supervisors ordered the
gas bills paid over the veto of the Mayor.
It was further stated that the Mutual
Company would seek to destroy the pres
ent gas contract in the courts on the
ground of illegality.
Supervisor Haskins was asked as to
what action the board would probably
take in relation to the veto. He replied:
"So far as I am concerned I don't care
what is done. It makes little difference
whether or not the veto is overridden."
Haskins is one of the nine who knocked
out the first gas contract veto.
Another matter which wa3 scheduled
for the meeting yesterday was the tinal
apportionment of the different depart
ment appropriations. Haskins, who is a
member of the Finance Committee having
the matter in charge, stated that it was
the intention to bring in a report making
no increase in the money to be devoted
to the Health Department. This news
will be somewhat of a surprise to the
Board of Health, as it was confidently
predicted that the Supervisors would ac
cede to the request for a larger allow
ance. To-morrow evening the Health

After all you never can tell what win
happen in this town on a Monday night.
I forwent the Frawleys last night in
what was doubtless a very decent per
formance of a Daly comedy to go over
the way to the Columbia and see the
Messrs. Mathews and Bulger in "By
the Sad Sea Waves." I hadn't been
there fifteen minutes before I knew
that my understudy had got the best of
it; but it was too late for an exchange
and I stayed it out till the last.
The Mean*. Mathews and Bulger
have, I admit, never been more impor
tant than the Frawley Company AND
Blanche Bates (the posters and pro
grammes very properly Insist on this
big "and"), but one wearies of writing
about even the Frawleys all the time.
Variety At the spice of life and a 6 last
season Mr. Mathews and Mr. Bulger
had given us the epice that is the life
of variety I did not have the courage
to stay away from them this time.
Perhaps you remember them in a piece
called "A Trip to Coney Island." or
6omething like that. It was a silly
piece, the very inflammation of Ameri
can farce comedy, but it was relent
lessly laughable. You went and you
laughed and lost your right to say
unpretty things about the show after
ward when your normal solemn mood
set in. Mathews. with his tireless pho
nographic persistence, and Bulger, with
his sublimated tramp comicality, and
the speed and patness of their gags, all
conspired to make you forget such
things as sanity and seriousness and I
dramatic criti<-i=Tn. And then there
was a pretty little girl who sang coon j
gongs with the real pumpkin-colored '
swagger and made herself a topic of ,
the town. For all I know she may still 1
be with the company, but if so a year's i
time has wrought terrible changes.
There was nothing in the cast last |
night that sounded or looked the ieast |
bit like her. And Mr. Ma thews and
Mr. Bulger didn't reeda like the same !
old smile. In the last act they did their
old specialty of rehearsing a play, and :
they repeated t*ie drum and trombone j
duet, and these episodes are still funny;
but the most of the rest of the time they
were dull and stale because of the i
piece, which is stupider than a New
York Casino "review," and relieved
at Intervals by a clever line or a
specialty of any moment.
One of these intervals is supplied by
Ned Weyburn. who does rag-time feats
on the piano. He is a wonder. He is,
if I am d â  ::.. erably rniÃtaken, the
com;: - Syncopated Sandy," the
most extraordinary double-twisted rag
time coon classic that ever was writ
ten, and he ought to be a wonder. He
is the only man I have ever heard ex
tract the genuine nigger essence from
juiceless ivories of a back parlor up
right. Actually he had the audacity
last night to play the "Wedding
March" in rag time.
I am usually satisfied with the
Mendelssohn version of this piece, but
once listen to the syncopated lilt that
Mr. Weyburn gives It and you will
agree with me that, born earlier. v
could have given Felix Barthoi^v
pointers on darktovrn movement and
humor. He also made "raÂ«s" of Mr.
De Korea'! "Promise Me." and the
"Star Spangled Banner," and wound ud
with, among other things in the med
ley, a snatch of "Syncopated Sandy."
until your ears yearned to hear some
plumped voiced May Irwin Bine it to
that accompaniment.
But, thank heaven, nobody in the
company sings to Mr. Weyburn's play
ing. With the exception of Miss Nellie
Hawthorne, who pipes a dainty little
legend about "The Willow Pattern
Plate," there seems to be no one In the
company who can sing with human re
sults. There are three young; women
â who aing a trio Ir. the same number of
keys, and there are vocal soloists whose
lives would not be secure on a Sunday
night at the Orpheum, and there is not
a good new song in the entire perform
ance.
Mathews and Bulger (especially Bul
ger) are clever fellows, but it ie only
in their old specialty act that they have
any chance. The hit of the show ie
Ned Weyburn. His ragtime convolu
tions commence at about 9:45.

The Baldwin.
Blanche Bates and Frawley are a well
matched couple in the reconciliation scene
In Valabreque's neat comedy, "Love In j
Harness," presented at the Baldwin last ]
night. %'Tls true. Miss Bates is rather i
tall for him, but Khe leans down upon his â 
shoulder as though she belonged there.
There is not much out of the common In
the role of Una Urquhart, the jealous j
and discontented wife of the play, but j
the actress has made a flesh and blood j
part of it. Where there was much temp- I
tat ion and opportunity to overact ehe
simply played the part as the audience j
at once realized that it should be playedâ
with human interest and action. The
comedy, while It lacks the uproarious ac
tion, the broad humor and many ridicu
lous situations of "The Gay Parisians," is
clean and neat. There are no sugges
tions of the improper, and the fun is in
nocent and naturally born.
"Love in Harness" is a probable piece,
not a miracle play, where the most as
tounding events occur by supernatural
agency and are taken ac a matter of
course by the actors and the audience.
Frawiey and Hickman played their parts
as the forsaken husbands quietly and
naturally, and the creases in their panta
â were f aultlefss. Mathleu has a very Bmall
ro leâin fact, that of a walking gentle
man, and it Is so small. Indeed, that it
does not matter much whether he plays
it well or Illy.
The women of the comedy were excel
lent, and the piece went off with a snap
and go that was most refreshing to the
first nighters.
The y\]Kambra.
A decided novelty was presented last
evening at the Alhambra. The manage
ment has secured as an attraction a Cu
ban orchestra and a company of Cubans
who give a unique little operetta entitled
"Artists from Matanzas," Illustrating In
a measure life In the little Island, In
which there is now so much trouble. The
music Is decidedly out of the ordinary
and the dancing Is captivating. The dia
logue and songs are in Spanish. The
five-act drama, "Over the Sea." founded
upon Charles Reade's novel, "Never Too
Late to Mend." was presented by a well
drilled company, which held the audi
ence until after U o'clock. The principal
parts are taken by Harry Jackson, "V\ . L.
Gleason, Charles Bates, Henry Napier.
Charles Hand. Carmeletla Yost and Mina
Gieason. There was one trio that re
ceived unstinted applause. Morelana,
Thompson and Roberts, vocalists and
dancers were called out half a dozen
times, and each specialty presented en
couraged their friends to demand more.
The evening's entertainment was intro
duced by Robert Downing and his com
pany in 'a "French Marriage."

The usual packed house greeted the new
bill at the Orpheum last night, and of the

Mrs. Edwin Milton Royle and their clever
company. No such vaudeville turn has ever
before been seen in this city as the play,
"Captain Impudence," by the Boyles.
The entire comedy, plot and action of the
play ha% - e been reduced from three acts

Milton Royle and Seier.a Fetter little need

No daintier, saucier little bunch of femi
ninity ever stepped on the Orpheum stage
than Mamie Dupont. who plays the part
of the Major's daughter, while the Major,
in the hands of William Frederic, was
skillfully pictured.
Lois M. Granat as a whistler excels.
She not only scored a hit but. firmly es
tablished herself as a favorite. F>:ix
and Barry have a skit that opens rather
tame, but warms up to a howling suc

very clever novelty act in which he corn
fancy rifle shooting with balancing,

act. Miss M;try N irraan holds her place as

The Circus.
Wallace's circus has caught the town. !
Nightly thousands of parents of this city.
accompanied by their little ones, sit under
the immense tent intently watching the ;
feats of agiiity and strength and applaud- (
Ing each number in such hearty manner
that the performers try to outdo each
other. The programme presented is one
of the best seen In this city in years and
contains many startling turns.
From the entrance of the company
troupe gayly caparisoned, to the exciting
hippodrome races, the interest is un
ceasing. As on the opening night the Nel
son family were well received. The evolu
tions performed by the youngest of tins
remarkable collection of athletes created
much enthusiasm. The work of the bare
back riders, trapeze performers and hur
dlers proved an excellent exhibition of
grace and daring and was loudly ap
plauded. The hippodrome races were of
the whirlwind order and the large crowd
were thrilled at the danger incurred by
those who participated In the events. Of
those the chariot race was the most ex
citing and the victor was cheered as In the
days of ancient Rome.
After the performance a concert was
given and was much enjoyed. The side
Bhow, with its numerous freaks and coi
lection of strange and uncanny things,
was crowded, and the large number of
animals on exhibition received much at
tention. Of these the monkeys and ele
phants fared the best. PeanutÃ and pop
corn were showered on them until even
their keepers feared that indigestion
would keep them awake during the night.
A performance Is given every afternoon
and evening to satisfy the large number
of people who are anxious to attend. The
three rings keep even' one busy, and It
is worth one'o while to visit Central Park.
Jhe O'.ympia.
The Olympia Music Hall has almost an
entire new bill. Cotton and Moll, triple
bar artists. F. Fredericks, lyric tenor,
and Rogers, a dashing soubrette, are all
new. Little Irene Franklin put on her
Impersonations of the waiters in a tough
restaurant, and made a hit. This Is the
act in which she scored a success upon
her first appearance at the Orpheum two
years ago. ,
The Chutes.
The bill at the Chutes Free Theater was
almost entirely new, and It went with a
snap and dash most refreshing. Kelly and
Violette, cake walkers and singers, Lil
lian Stewart, the "tough girl," and Mason
and Linsley. triple horizontal bar perform
ers, made especial hits. A decided nov
elty was the introduction of portraits of
the municipal candidates in the trans
formation dance, and a moving picture
showing the Republican nominees was
waxmly greeted. The house was packed.

Mr. and Mrs. Max Heinrich, barytone
and soprano, and Julia Heinrich, contral
to, will give their first recital In this city
at Sherman and Clay Hall this even
ing. They are renowned as ballad and
classic singers, and their first appearance
will be hailed with great interest by local
music lovers.

THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, OCTOBER 18, 1898^
ELEVEN JURORS
DECLARE NEAD
TO BE INNOCENT
Oneof the Panel Held
Out for Conviction.
THE PRISONER BROKE DOWN
ALLOWED TO GO ON HIS OWN
RECOGNIZANCE.
Nead Considers the Ballot of the
Jury to Be Virtually a
Verdict in His
Favor.
A verdict of eleven to one In favor of
! acquittal was the result of the trial of
: John M. Nead for vagrancy, just con
i eluded before Judge Conlan.
The prosecution closed its case after
; the examination of three witnesses had
! been sworn. The first of these was Hat
l tie Roberts, with whom it had been stat
! Ed Xead and his wife had been seen in
i the Thalia. Following her came Gus
i Welter, who testified to having been
'â  spoken to by Mrs. Nead and to having ac
' companied her home. Mrs. Nead herself
was the last witness.
Mrs. Nead was asked how long she had
b*en in the city and then if she knew the
; defendant. "Yes, he is my husband," she
said. Mr. Lemon, prosecuting counsel,
then announced that under that state
ment he would ask no further questions.
Mr. Gallagher then waived any right to
remain siler.t that she had as the wife of
the accused, and the court also took a
hand and quieted the rather heated ar
gument the lawyers were getting into.
Judge Conlan asked the witness if she
â¢waived any rights she had and she said j
she did ao. The prosecution was told to j
go on with its questions. Mr. Lemon j
wanted to ask only one, howeverâ "Do j
you live at 125 Turk street?" The wit- j
admitted that she did, and then the j
prosecution declared Its case closed.
Mrs. Nead was the first witness for the i
defense. She was asked if she had done ;
aii the things she is accused of doing and j
she denied each as it was enumerated to
her. She also denied knowing the peo- j
pie who had so positively identified herâ .
Teasfe Smith, Hattie Roberts the police |
officers and the two women who said she
had lived in their houses, and the men j
who had testified to having been spoken
to by her in the street. She denied also j
being in the Thalia on any occasion.
Frank Bell and C. A. Reynolds, her
own attorney and the prosecuting attor
ney of Police Court No. 4 were called to ;
impeach the record of her conviction in
that court, but this was not allowed.
Nead fcimseif was then called. He told
how ho went to the Golden Eagle Hotel
when he and his wife first came to the
city and how after living there for a
week they bought a little store on Mar
ket street. They sold this out in the
Latter part of July, and since then he
Bald he had been making his living by
selling religious books. He submitted two
authorizations to exhort from the First
Methodist Church of Sioux City. lowa, \
signed by the pastor of the church G .v . |
v Brown. One of these was dated July.
U96 and the other November, 1597. A tele- 'â 
gram to the Mayor and one to the Chief
of Police, both of which have been pub
lished here, -were aiso allowed in evidence
against the protest of the prosecution.
The court ruled, however, that anything
that would tend to help the case of the
defendant was admissible. Nead was then
h-d through a long list of denials similar
to those made by Mrs. Nead.
The most important part of his testi
mony was in regard to the events of the
night of September 23, on which It is
charged that Mrs. Nead spoke to Poilce
man Joy on the street. *>cad said his
wife was sick all that afternoon and
evening and that she did not leave the
house. He said he remembered it because
he had put it down in his diary, and then
he was asked to show the diary. He did
so and was rigidly cross-examined by Mr
Lennon and made some additions and
erasures. The book was finally ad
mitted in evidence. It served as the basis
on which Lennon attacked the testimony
of both the defendant and his wife.
It had been shown by one or the wit
nesses for the prosecution that in the
evening Nead appeared with a light mus
tache and in the evening he was clean
shaven or had a dark mustache. This
Nead denied, and he added that he never
dyed his mustache or wore a false one. He
said the only times he had ever been to
the Thalia was after the arrest of his
wife and then he went down there to see
what kind of a place it was, as he had
heard that there were to be witnesses
from there. _
Several witnesses were then called to
testify to the good character held by
Nead and his wife, and the case closed.
The attorneys wanted to argrue the case,
and Mr. Lemon to save time waived his
opening address. To this Mr. Gallagher
objected, but the court allowed it, and for
an hour the case was argued pro and con.
After btlng out over two hours the Jury
failed to arrive at a verdict and was dis
charged It is understood that the jurors
stood eleven for acquittal and one for
conviction. Judge Conlan at once ordered
the defendant released on his own recog-
Xead broke down and wept bltterly
when the foreman of the jury announced
that it was impossible to agree on a ver
dict As the jurors filed out of the court
room Nead, with tears streaming down
his cheeks shook hands with several of
them. The wife, who was also crying,
led him from the court room, after vainly
endeavoring to cheer him up.
A SLAP SUGGESTS DEATH.
A Family Quarrel IMves Kate Pan
chella to Attempt Suicide.
Kate Panchella, residing at 21 Ohio
street, attempted suicide last evening be
taking a dose of iodine. The reason of
her desire to die was a quarrel with her
husband, during which he slapped her.
The husband, Joe Panchella, a horse
shoer, says that this is the third attempt
his wife has made to kill herself. She
left home a few days ago, while drink
ing heavily, but was persuaded to return
by her husband. An effort was made to
settle the differences between the two and
the attempt was so successful that the
husband finally gave her some money
with which to buy trinkets as a peace
offering. In discussing the trouble a
quarrel resulted, in which Panchella slap
ped his wife. She went to a neighboring
drug store and purchased the tincture of
Mrs. Panchella was taken to the Hafbor
Receiving Hospital, where she was treat
ed by Dr. Fitzgibbon. Up to a late hour
last night she was still unconscious from
the effects of the lodine, but the physi
cians believe that she will recover.

trying a "Koh-I-Noor" copying pencil. â¢
MBS. DANIELL'S WOES.
Rev. John Hemphill Summoned to
England to Aid His Step-
Daughter.
Rev. John Hemphlll, pastor of Calvary
Presbyterian Church, is on his way to
England to render what assistance lies in
j his power to his stepdaughter, Mrs. James
| W. Danlell, who is experiencing no end of
trouble with her aristocratic husband.
Dr. Hemphlll started a week ago to-day
at an hour's notice, but his absence was
not generally known until last Sunday,
when Rev. S. 8. Kerr filled his pulpit.
Mrs. HemDhill went to England several
weeks ago to assist her daughter, and it
was the receipt of an urgent telegram
from her that started Dr. pemphlll off so
suddenly.
The marital troubles of Mrs. Daniell
have long been the subject of gossip on
both sides of the Atlantic. She is the
daughter of Mrs. Hemphlll by a former
marriage, her father's name being Cog
hill. Miss Coghill married R. P. Hastings, j
son of the late Judge S. C. Hastings by
whom she had two children. After her nus
band's death she went to England and m
ISA was married to Daniell. a member or
an aristocratic English family. Â« ne â¢
Hastings died he left his entire fortune to
the widow, making no provision for tne
children. There was a bitter contest over
this, and the widow compromised by set
tling half the property upon the two chil
dren, who were allowed $600 a month oy
the Superior Court here. , Â«_.
Daniell has squandered his wife s for
tune and Is making desperate efforts to
gain possession of his stepchildren s in
heritance, and it is to prevent this tna. |
Dr. Hemphill has gone to England, tan- .
ing a lawyer with him. Mrs. Daniel has
refused to seek a divorce so far. creaa
ing the notoriety, but she will begin
action at once for a legal separation.
Ministers' Meeting.
Rev Dr. F. B. Cherington read a sharp
criticism on Mme. Blavatsky before the
Congregational ministers' meeting jester- !
day. He outlined the career of the
madame during her stay in India and her ;
ultimate exposure through a Frenchman ,
named Coulomb. The paper was careful'.y
prepaxed and its reading was intently lis
tened to. A number of Interesting talks
were delivered before the Methodist min

AMENDMENT NUMBER FIVE.
(Being Assembly Constitutional Amendment
No. 36.)
A resolution to propose to the people of the
State of California an amendment of section
atteen and section sixteen of article five of the
Constitution of the State of California, by
which it is proposed to amend eald sections to
read as follows:
Section IS. A Lieutenant Governor shall be
elected at the same time and place and in the
same manner as the Governor, and his term of
office and his qualifications shall be the same.
He shall be president of the Senate, but shall
only have a casting vote therein. -
Section 16. In caee of the impeachment of
the Governor, or his removal from office, death,
inability to discharge the powers and duties of
bis office, resignation or absence from the
State, the powers and duties of the office shall
devolve upon the Lieutenant Goven.or for the
residue of the term, or until the disability shaU
cease. And should the Lieutenant Governor be
Impeached, displaced, resign, die or become In-
capable of performing the duties of his office,
or be absent frcm the State, the president pro
tempore of the Senate shall act as Governor un-
til the vacancy In the office of Governor shall
be filled at the next general election when
members of the Legislature shall be chosen, or
until such disability of the Lieutenant Gov-
ernor shall cease. In case of a vacancy In the
office of Governor for any of the reasons above
named, and neither the Lieutenant Governor
nor the president pro tempore of the Senate
shall succeed to the powers and duties of Gov-
ernor, then the powers and duties of such of-
fice shall devolve upon the speaker of the As-
sembly, until the office of Governor shall be
filled at such general election.
AMENDMENT NUMBER SIX.
(Being Assembly Constitutional Amendment
No. SS.)
A resolution to propose to the people of the
State of California an amendment to section
six. article nine of the Constitution of the State
of California, relating to grammar schools, by
which it is proposed to amend said section to
read as follows:
Section 6. The public school system shall In-
clude primary and grammar schools, and such
high schools, evening schools, normal schools
and technical schools as may be established by
the Legislature or by municipal or district
authority, but the entire revenue derived frora
the State school fund and the State school tax
shall be applied exclusively to the support of
primary and grammar schools. Grammar
schools ehall Include schools organized in a
school district, or union of school districts,
having more than one thousand Inhabitants, in
; which a course of study shall be taught which
; will prepare pupils to enter the agricultural,
mining or scientific department of the Univer-
sity of California.

(Being Assembly Constitutional Amendment

A resolution to propose to the people of the

Section t. The sessions of the Legislature
shall commence at twelve o'clock meridian on

nor more than sixty days from the time of ad-

session, the Governor shall, by proclamation,
fix a date for such reconvening, which shall be
within the limits above prescribed. Upon re-
assembling the Legislature shall complete its
session. ' No pay shall be allowed to member*

no bill shall be Introduced in erther houÂ«Â» exÂ»
cept at the first twenty-SÂ»e days of the session,

+ IS9S ..........:....... f 37,804Â«qTO KEEP LEPROSY
FROM OUR SHORES
State Board of Health
Will Act.
MISSION OF DR. RUGGLES
GOES TO HAWAII TO CONDUCT
HTVESTIGATIONS.
The Board Considers the Presence of
Texas Fever and Will Recom
mend Measures to Stamp
It Out.
SjÂ»eclal Dispatch to ThÂ» Call.
SACRAMENTO, Oct. 17.â At a meet-
Ing of the State Board of Health at the
Capitol to-nisrht Dr. C. A. Rugbies,
president of the board, was appointed
4o visit the Hawaiian Islands to learn
the extent of the presence of leprosy
M-nd to report measures to prevent its
introduction into California. Dr. J. K.
Morse has been appointed for this mis
jtforse had been appointed or this mis
could fulfill it. Since the annexation of
â¢Hawaii its inhabitants are free to come
to these ports, and It is the intention of
the Board of Health of prepare a re
port for submission to the coming Leg
islature which will suggest safeguards
against the spread of the disease to
these shores.
Dr. Ruggles informed The Call cor
respondent that he would sail on No
vember S. Before his departure Dr.
Huggles will join Drs. Matthews, Hen
derson and Crowley in an investigation
of the Capitol building with regard to
sanitation. Dr. Ruggles asserted to
night that undoubtedly lives had been
lost and other lives threatened by the
lack of sanitation in the big white
-structure. The report of the Board of
Health on this subject Is expected to
form the basis for action by the next
Legislature.
Dr. Mathews. secretary of the board,
ted that Texas fever existed to a
â wide extent among cattle in this State.
He said that when the thermometer
reached 30 decrees the fever tick would
die, thus freeing the cattle in the north
ern portion of the State from the dis
ease. It will then become the duty of
the board next spring to draw a line
through the State, separating the in
fected portion from that from which
the cold weather has driven the dis
ease, and the quarantine must be strict.
Dr. Mathews said the reports con
cerning anthrax had been exaggerated,
and in several instances where it was
thought to have been discovered it was
found that the real cause of death was
insufficient food and impure water due
ht.
The Board of Health will ask the
Legislature for an enlargement of
its powers in the matter of handling
anthrax and similar diseases, including
authority to appoint veterinarians, who
shall go- to infected localities and send
ports, and who shall have the
ITiviiege of destroying cattle found to
be diseased. As it is now, the State
board has no province in the matter,
the counties alone handling it, and
the only way the evil can be reached, in
most of them, is by citizens going be
fore the District Attorneys and swear
ing to complaints against the owners
of infected stock.
The board adjourned to meet to-mor
row night in San Francisco. Several
days will be spent in inspecting the
sanitary condition of the Oakland
Home for the Blind and the Normal
School and other State institutions
around San Francisco.
No Fever.
SAN JOSE, Oct. 17.â The letter from
the San Francisco Board of Health to
the San Jose Board of Health, inquir
ing about the Texas fever among cat
tle, was referred to the Board of Su
! --.-visors. It was presented to-day to
the Supervisors, but as the general
idea seemed to be that there was no
fever among cattle in this county, the
letter went to the file without action.
NEEDLESS EXPENSE
IN SHIPPING WOOD
SANTIAGO DE CUBA, Oct. 17.â The
wood used by the military establish
ment for Government purposes since
the capitulation of Santiago has been
sent here from the United States at a
cost of $12 a cord, counting the expense
of transportation, of labor in loading
and unloading cars, shipping and un
ehipping. This has been a needless
â¢waste of money. The forests are full
of wood of all descriptions, thoroughly
suitable to all the Government uses.
The matter was brought to the notice
of Major Knight, the chief quartermas
ter, who is now inviting tenders from
firms, and will probably get all
he requires at about $4 a curd.
The schoor Golden Rod arrived from
Jamaica with 63 negroes on board. They
have not been allowed to land, but will
be sent back. General Wood is deter
mined to stop the spread of unneces-
Bary destitution in the province, and he
thinks one of the best precautions
would be to prevent such immigrations.
Mr. Mason, the British Consul, has for
maliy protested again.st General Wood's
attitude, though he recognizes the wis
dom of the policy. General Wood has
offered to allow immigrants from Ja
macia to land providing the British
Consuls guarantees that none will be
come destitute. Under such conditions
a few will be admitted.
â  ial Sanchez of the Cuban army,
ipanled by five members of his
arrived to-day and visited Gen
eral Wood. They are a fine looking lot
n, by far the best looking Cu
et at Santiago. A reception
will be tendered them to-night at the
Club.
TIMES OF DEPARTURE
OF SPANISH TROOPS
HAVANA, Oct. 17.â The American
military commission held a conference
at their headquarters in the Trocha
Hotel to-day. Rear Admiral Sampson
was present, .enor Giranta, secretary
of the Spanish Commission, presented
to the American Commissioners an of
ficial note setting forth the time when
the next detachment of Spanish, troops
will leave the island.
It is reported that many officers of
General Gomez' staff have received
leave of absence, but it i s added they
have really resigned, owinVto serioua
trouble with their chief. It is father
asserted that the only etaff officers are
Deschapele, a Dominican, and Mariel
Alva, a colored Dominican, and Marcos
Rosario. Among those who have left
Gomez, according to the report, are Dr.
Fermin, Valdez, Dominguez, his chief
of staff; Major Boza, Surgeon Lucas,
Alvarez and Aides de Camp Tafel, Es
trampes and Molina. They say that
Gomez, who is always bad tempered,
has lately become unreasonable, mak
ing it Impossible to remain by his side.
The City Council at its session of
Saturday last decided to charge the
American military commanders $12 BO
each for six plans of the city of Ha
vana, furnished to them at their re
quest.
SPANISH COMMISSION
GETS SHARP RETORT
LONDON, Oct. 18.â The Paris corre
spondent of the Times says: At the
sitting of the Peace Commission this
(Monday) afternoon the American
commissioners officiallyâ and it would
seem definitelyâ rejected the idea of
accepting sovereignty over Cuba for
the United States. The Spanish com
missioners thereupon remarked that
Spain, having abandoned such sov
ereignty under pressure of the United
States, and the United States having
denied that the sovereignty henceforth
belongs to her, Cuba is de facto in a
state of independency, as an intermis
sion of sovereignty cannot be viewed In
any other light.
The American commissioners rejoin
ed that, without accepting sovereignty,
the United States considered itself
deputed to maintain security for all
the inhabitants, that they would not
fail to introduce and support, and they
would do all that was necessary to put
the island in a state of normal organi
zation to be transformed at the earli
est moment into a legal regularity,
satisfactory for the security of aIL
Upon this the Spanish commission
ers with what seems striking logic,
promptly replied that by the fact of
the declaration just made to them and
of the United States undertaking to
restore legality, order and security, the
United States was invested with
sovereignty and consequently could not
refuse to accept the consequences of
such sovereignty, seeing that they ad
mittedly could not tolerate the relapse
into anarchy of an island placed un
der their supervision and direction.
Notwithstanding these striking objec
tions, the Americans persisted in their
refusal and officially declared that the
United States cannot be con
sidered to be invested by sovereignty
and they could ni_rt deviate from this
derinite declaration. According to im
pressions I have been able to collect in
various circles, unconnected with either
side, but entitled to weight, the refusal
of the Americans is not of a positive
character as would seem to result from
the formal declaration mentioned. The
reason given me is this:
The United States have declared that
they made a war not of conquest, but
of liberation and order, because they
could not allow the propagation at
their very doors of a state of things
which was, in their eyes, a blow to the
cause of humanity and civilization.
They, therefore, made a public declara
tion that the war was not one of con
quest, and they now consider that by
agreeing to be invested with sover
eignty of Cuba they would give them
selves the appearance of having con
quered the island for territorial ag
grandizement.
They refuse to give themselves the
appearance of a conquering nation.
Hence they positively refuse to accept
that charge of sovereignty which would
be inconsistent with the character of |
humanitarian disinterestedness and es- j
sential to the honor of America.
The United States comprehend that
their firm refusal to accept sovereignty j
does not exclude obligation, on the i
footing of justice and equity, to make j
Spain real concessions as to financial j
burdens which would be crushing if she
were saddled with the whole Cuban |
debt. We may be certain that on this j
point the Americans will be less inflex
ible than on the people of society. The j
proof, I am told, that the United States !
are disposed to meet Spain in an equi- I
table fashion is that the Spanish Com- }
missioners, who would not at iirst ac- !
cept any invitation to dine with Gen- |
eral Horace Porter until the end of the
conference, have now accepted.
The Paris correspondent of the Daily !
Mail says: I hear that the peace prob- '
lem will soon be settled, the United I
States assuming the Cuban 6 per cent !
loan of 18S6, which was floated entirely j
for Cuban purposes, and rejecting the
5 per cent loan of 1890.
A dispatch to the London News ,
Agency from Paris says: To-day ;
(Monday) the conference reached a >
crisis for the first time. Judge Day pre- \
sented the demands of the American
Commission in threatening words. He !
said that delay was the only object of I
the Spanish Commissioners to saddle j
the United States with the Cuban debt, I
and would be tolerated no longer, as j
the United States would neither assume
nor guarantee any part of the debt.
The Spaniards replied that this i
placed Spain in a position of repudiat- \
ing or of reducing the face value of the i
Cuban bonds from 50 to 60 per cent, !
paying only half the stipulated interest j
on the reduced value. Before they
adopted either alternative they would
surrender to the United States the en
tire Philippines.
Judge Day responed that the surren
der of the Philippines would probably
be demanded irrespective of the Cuban
or any other debt.
This, to the Spaniards, the first in
timation of the intentions of the United
States as to the Philippines resulted in
a whispered conference, followed by an
adjournment in order to communicate
with the Madrid Government. Judge
Day said that President McKlnley had
instructed him to demand the entire
surrender of Porto Rico to-morrow
(Tuesday).
FUTURE GOVERNMENT
OF PORTO RICO ISLAND
WASHINGTON, Oct. 17.â General
Brooke has plenary powers in Porto
Rico and will have sole charge of the
government of the island until some
other arrangement is made. It is pos
sible that when the President returns
he may designate a military govern
ment of the island to act in that capac
ity until Congress provides for a terri
torial civil government. No instruc
tions have been sent to General Brooke
regarding his duties after the war when
the island formally passes over to the
United States and all Spanish author
ity is withdrawn, but he is acquainted
with the proclamations of the President
regarding the management of affairs.
General Brooke, it Is understood, in
tends to return to the United States as
soon as the affairs of the island become
more settled, but probably before that
time a military government will be
named, as was done at Santiago.
CONCESSIONS MADE
BY GENERAL BLANCO
HAVANA, Oct. 17.â This afternoon
Senor Benitz, assistant secretary of the
Spanish evacuation commission, de
livered to the United States evacuation
commissioners five notes, four of which
treated on the fortifications of the is
lunds.
The fifth note asserted that Captain
General Blanco formally acceded to the
request of the American commission to
permit the free entry of Red Cross sup
plies at the ports of Havana, Cardenas,
Clenfuegoe, Sagua la Grande, Calbal
ren, Bahia Honda and others of less
Importance.

ENCAMPMENT OF
THE ODD FELLOWS

SAN JOSE, Oot 17.â Delegates to the
forty-fourth Grand Encampment of the

REFUTES SOME OF
SEAMAN'S TESTIMONY
Subsistence Department Issues a
Statement Concerning Sup
plies for Sick
Soldiers.
WASHINGTON. Oct. 17.â The subsist
ence department of the army has issued a
statement refuting some features of the
testimony of Major Seaman, surgeon, be
fore the War Investigating Commission.
Major Seaman said no one could get sup
plies for the sick from the commissary
office, yet when he left Ponce, the chief
commissary there had at his disposal
$44,000 subject to requisition of surgeons
for just such supplies as Seaman said he
wanted.
Major Daly, surgeon, who came with
the troops about the same time, made a
requisition and secured $3600 for the pur
chase of supplies for the sick and turned
the unexpended balance Into the depart
ment here when he arrived.
CANONS RELATING
TO REMARRIAGE STAND
Propositions in Begturd to Divorced
Persons Voted Down by Episco
pal House of Bishops.
WASHINGTON, Oct. 17.â Important ac
tion was taken by thÂ« respective bodies
of the Episcopalian council here to-day
on two subjects which have caused much
discussion during the deliberations of
those two bodies.
The House of Bishops, by a votÂ« of
31 to 32 rejected propositions bearing on the
subject of remarriage of divorced per
sons designed to take the place of those
now in existence. The present canons on
that subject, therefore, remain in force.
In the House of Deputies, after a de
bate which has continued for several
days, a step In the direction of church
unity was taken. The discussion of this
subject was precipitated by the offering
of an amendment to the constitution by
the Rev. Dr. Huntington of New York.
While agreeing with the principle of this
the deputies finally decided to adopt a
briefer amendment offered by Mr. Faude-*
of Minnesota, as follows:
"But provision may be made by canon
for the temporary use of other forms and
directories of worship by congregations
not already in union with this church,
who are willing to accept the spiritual
oversight of the Bishop of the diocese."
The amendment, if adopted by the
Bishops, will go to the dioceses for ac
tion and must be passed by the next gen
eral convention before it becomes opera
tive.
YELLOW FEVER WILL
NOT STAY OCCUPATION
WASHINGTON, Oct 17.â While the
wet season has now almost reached its
end in Cuba, it is said that yellow fever
Is more prevalent and more dangerous
in type just now than at any other time
during the year. These conditions,
however, will not continue long, and it
is believed that before November has
progressed far it will be possible to
have several divisions of troops safely
camped on Cuban soil. A proposition
that is now under consideration looks
to the employment of the Cubans as
local gendarmes, it being urged that
they would be more serviceable In
maintaining order in the towns than
American soldiers, unacquainted with
the Spanish language and Cuban cus
toms.
The United States Government In this
way would be able by the payment of
small salaries to these men to consid
erably relieve the distress that exists
on the island without breeding a class
of professional paupers or lowering the
pride of the Cubans.
Unless General Lee specifically asks
to be relieved from that duty, he is to
command the first expedition, seconded
by General Greene and General Arnold.
TRANSPORTATION OF
CHRISTMAS GIFTS
Quartermaster's Department Will
Deliver Packages to Soldiers
at Manila.
NEW YORK, Oct. 17.â A dispatch to
the Tribune from Washington says: The
Quartermaster's Department of the army
is arranging to extend its good offices in
the direction of transporting a reasonable
quantity of Christmas gifts to the Amer
ican soldiers in garrison at Manila. It
will be necessary to start a steamer from
San Francisco early in November in or
der to reach the Philippines by Christmas,
and the friends of the soldier boys out be
yond the Pacific who wish to forward
boxes of grood thingrs are already in corre
spondence with the quartermaster general
on the subject.
The rigid rule has been laid down that
Christmas boxes for the soldiers at Ma
nila must not contain quantities of sweet
things calculated to disturb the digestion
of men living in the tropics. Mince meat,
fruit cake and candy will be permitted
only in small parcels, and it is advisable
that packages be limited in weight, as
the department will not be able to for
ward over 1000 tons at the utmost. The
packages will have to be delivered at San
Francisco without expense to the Gov
ernment, which has no fund for trans
porting- such supplies, and It Is essential
that the quartermaster general shall
give permission In each case for the pack
age to go aboard a Government transport.
CONSPIRACY TO MURDER
ALLEGED BY NEVILLS
Mine-Owner Brings a New Suit
Against the Azhderian-Wil
liams-Loose Trio.
FRESNO, Oct. 17.â Captain Nevills has
again begun proceedings against the
Azhderian-Williams-Loose trio, although
just what his purpose is this time is not
quite clear. Some time ago they were ar
rested for attempted extortion and sev
eral cases of alleged grand larceny, but
nothing came of the charges. The extor
tion charge was tried before Judge Joach
imsen of San Francisco, and the defend
ants were discharged. Other cases here
were dismissed by the District Attorney.
To-day Nevills had Azhderian and the
two women arrested for a conspiracy to
murder. They gave bail before Justice
St John in the sum of $500 each. The case
is an old one, and tho facts upon which it
is based have been published several
times in connection with the troubles of
the wealthy mine-owner.
Mrs. Williams has a charge pending in
the Federal court against Nevills, whom
she accuses of having sent obscene letters
to her through the mail.
BRUSH FIRES IN
MONTEREY COUNTY
Three Conflagrations That Bid Fair
to Cause Considerable
Loss.
MONTEREY, Oct. 17.â Three fires have
been raging in this locality for the past
twenty-four hours, and the temperature
is, therefore, unusually .high. The near
est of tho fires is in the Sansito dis
trict near the county road between this
nlace and Salinas, on land owned by
David Jacks, and unless it is soon con
trolled it will result in considerable loss
of fences and cattle feed. Mr. Jacks has
had men fighting it since yesterday
morning, and unceasing work is gradually
netting the better of the flames.
Another fire is in the brush in C.armel
Valley ami a third in the chaparral in
the vicinity of Point Lobos. Both of these
are large and will be dangerous if allowed
to burn unchecked. The atmosphere is
full of smoke and the odor of burning
sage and other brush.

REFUSES TO RETTTRN IT.
Colonel John R. Berry Holding
Money Desired by the Red
Cross Society.
The ladies of the Red Cross and Colonel
John R. Berry, commanding the Seventh
California Regiment, have had a slight
misunderstanding regarding the posses
sion of $522, which is causing the ladies
some little worry and incidentally result
ing in their saying some rather sharp
things about the colonel. He refuses to
give up the money, the proceeds of a drill
given by his regiment at the desire of the
State Red Cross Society, when his regi
ment was a part of the expeditionary
forces. Colonel Berry claims that the
money was the property of the regiment
without any conditions being attached to
it, while the ladies claim that it was an
emergency fund for the use of the regi
ment in Manila. Now that the regiment
will not go to Manila the ladies think
that the money should be returned to tho
society, but it is evident that the colonel
thinks differently.
The ladies tried to get the money before
the regiment left the city, but Colonel
Berry promised to settle the matter at his
convenience when he reached Los An
geles. According to a member of the ex
ecutive committee of the Red Cross So
ciety legal proceedings may be instituted
if Colonel Berry does not return the
money.

SANTA FE OVERLAND WRECKED
Train Barely Escapes a Flange Down
the Mountain.
LOS ANGELES, Oct. 17.â The Santa Fe
overland westbound was wrecked yester
day at Summit station, on the Cajon Pass
grade, but no serious damage was done,
except to the engine, which had the front
end stove in. A number of men were at
work removing a trestle and substituting
an embankment, and the engine ran into
a quantity of dirt they had piled on the
track. It toppled over against the moun
tain side. Had it gone the other wav the
train would have fallen 200 feet. Mail
Agents Hanna and Bingjong were badly
shaken.

LINCOLN'S FONDNESS FOR
GRANT.
An amusing and possibly instructive
anecdote, in which Lincoln and Grant
figue, and showing the latter's estimate
of cavalry, is related by William O.
Stoddard. for some time one of the for
mer's private secretaries. The general
had not been long in command of the
Army of the Potomac, when one day
Mr. Stoddard asked Lincoln's opinion
of him. "Grant," replied the President,
"is the first general I've had. He's a
general!" Remembering the high es
teem in which McClellan, Buraside,
Hooker and Meade had been held, Mr.
Stoddard asked Lincoln to explain, and
this is what he said:
"You see, when any of the rest set
out on a campaign they'd look over
matters and pick out some one thing
they were short of, and they knew I
couldn't give 'em, and tell me they
couldn't hope to win unless they had it;
and It was most generally cavalry.
Now, when Grant took hold, I was
waiting to see what his pet Impossibil
ity would be, and I reckoned it would
be cavalry, as a matter of course, for
we hadn't horses enough to mount even
what men we had. There were 15,000
or thereabouts up near Harpers Ferry,
and no horses to put them on. Well,
the other day, just as I expected, Grant
Bent to me for those very men; but
what he wanted to know was whether
he could disband 'em or turn 'em into
infantry! He doesn't ask me to do im
possibilities for him, and he's the first
general I've had that didn't."
IT GAVE HIM THE BENDS.
Notwithstanding the danger, there
are always plenty of men to be had
at the water works tunnel. It Isn't
so easy, however, to keep them at
work. They are apt to tire of it very
soon, and all sorts of reasons are given
for quitting.
Not long ago a stout colored man ap
plied to the contractors for a job. He
was a well-built, intelligent looking
fellow, and the foreman at once en
gaged him. He was given charge of
a mule, and started in on his duties.
Not until the third day did he show
any objection to the work.
Then he came into the foreman's
room.
"Boss," ho said. "I gotter quit dis
job."
"What's the trouble?" Inquired the
foreman.
"Well, I jest cain't Btand it no
longer."
"What's wrong?"
"Well, boss, it's dat mule. I nevei
see no such cantankerous mule as dat
ar' one."
"What's the matter with the mule?"
"Mattah wld it? Well, if dat ain't
de wickedest mule dat ever walked on
two legs, den I don't know. Dats an
awdacious mule. Good Lor*, boss, dat
mule has done kick me in every part
of my physogomy. Dat'g right, an' I
ain't goin' to take no more of it."
"Did the mule kick you?"
"Did de mule kick me?"
The colored man echoed the fore
man's words in a tone expressive of the
most withering sarcasm. Then he
placed his hands across the pit of his
stomach and stooped forward.
"Say, boss," he cried, with painful
intensity, "dat llngblasted mule done
kick me so hard right heah dat he give
me de 'bends' foh shuah!"
Then the foreman paid him off and
let him go. â Cleveland Plain Dealer.
HOW THE VENETIANS TALK
FISH.
The streets of Burano are bordered
with massively built arcades, under the
shade of which women knit and spin,
and men wrangle in loud voices in a
dialect from which all consonants seem
to have been dropped. The same end
less argument concerning fish is for
ever in progress at Chioggia. I never
could learn precisely the nature of this
argument, but I am inclined to think
that it has its origin iq the congen
ital inability of a fisherman to tell the
truth as to the fish caught, or lost, by
him. I have never yet seen a Chiog
gian who was not just on the point
of shakiner the dust of the town from
his bare feet and going on board his
fishing-boat as the only refuge from
the unspeakable mendacity and general
villainy of his incredulous fellow
townsmen; or who had not Just landed
from a fishing excursion and was in
the act of taunting the rest of the world
with its inability to rival him as a pis
catorial liar.
The people of Venice affect to believe
that the men of Chioggia are even more
dangerous and quarrelsome than those
of Burano, but I have always found
both Chioggians and Buranese peace
ful and inoffensive folk, and the dis-^
like which the Venetians have for them
is doubtless due to their superior pow
ers of argument in regard to fish. â "W.
I*. Alden in Cassell's Magazine.
THE BABY "HAD BEEN USED."
Johnny is six years old, and he lives
on a North Side street. Just off from
Lincoln Park. And he is a wise boy
for one of his years.
A little sister arrived at the house
not long ago. Johnny was sent to the
grocery store on an errand a few days
later, and the proprietor began to quiz
him about it.
"I hear you have a little sister at
your house," said the grocer.
"Yes, sir," said Johnny.
"Do you like that?" was queried.
"I wish it was a boy," said Johnny,
"so I could play 'mibs' with him an'
baseball an' tag. an' all those things
when he got bigger."
"Well," said the storekeeper, "why
don't you exchange your little sister for
a boy?"
Johnny reflected for a minute. Then
he said, rather sorrowfully:
"We can't now. It's too late. "We've
used her four days." â Chicago Journal.
HOTTENTOT LULLABIES.
Hottentot women, who have long been
supposed to be about the lowest type
of human beings, have an extensive lit
erature of folk stories, folk songs and
baby songs, with which they amuse their
children. Many of the stories and songs
are of considerable beauty, and seem to
show that in some forgotten period the
tribe or race was much higher in the
scale of civilization than it is at the
present time.

WEATHEIi CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The weather ie cloudy over the northern por
tion of the Pacific Slope and generally clear
ever the southern half.
The pressure has risen along the northern
coast and fallen Quite rapidly over the plateau
and Rocky Mountain regions.
The temperature has risen slightly over Cali
fornia and Nevada and fallen slightly over
Oregon and Washington.
Light showers have fallen in Western Wash
ington and Northwestern Oregon.
Conditions are favorable for fair weather In
California Tuesday.

EASTERN MARKETa
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, Oct. 17.â Although the opening
was slightly higher in sympathy with the rise
In London, the tendency of prices turned down
ward early In the day. The coalers were sub
jected to attack by the bears, and the valuation
of the stocks lowered to an extent surprising
even for these much belabored securities. New
Jersey Central fell an extreme 5H and the de
cline in the rest of the group ranged from 1%
to 3. About the same time Metropolitan Street
Railway broke 3 1 * points, and the whole list
touched the lowest prices of the day. None of
the active railroad stocks fell over % point net.
London was a buyer in this market, especially
of the Pacifies. The covering of the bears In
the coalers worked recoveries ranging up to
2Vt on New Jersey Central, and the news that
there was no decision by the United States Su
preme I'ourt in the Joint Traffic Association
case Induced covering by room traders in the
general llFt. Manhattan was strong and Ha
waiian Commercial advanced at one time 6%.
The closing was firm at the top. There was a
notable firmness In the exchange market, not
withstanding very heavy offerings of bills
against grain and cotton.
Railroad bonds weakened in sympathy with
stocks, but showed a partial recovery toward
the close. Total sales. $l.."il0.O00.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, Oct. 17.â Large sales for export
to-day and report of damage to crop in Mani
toba and Australia caused a sharp rally In
wheat prices after an early decline. Decem
ber closed %C higher. Corn and oats also
responded to influence of Inclement weather
1 and scored good advance, December corn and
1 May oats gaining * Â©%c each. Pork lost 2%c,
lard 5c and ribs 2%@5c.
Stormy weather throughout the "West and
| Northwest, a little uneasiness over the war
'â  situation abroad and reports from the sea
i board of a continued good export demand for

i ktts abroad and fears of a large increase in
the visible supply which were afterward vexl
! fied caused a period of uneasiness shortly after
I the opening. There was a flood of bullish news

werp, which is considerably above the highest

! room. Minneapolis sent reports that her fl(Xir

kot strone, and that the Manitoba crop had

toba finally carried December up to 64%0 near J

THE STOCK MARKET.
Mining stocks were a shade lower, under the
usual dull business.
In local securities, Hawaiian advanced to
$44 I i and Hutchinson to $62V4, the former being
active. The other securities ranged about the
same.
At the annual meeting of the Eureka Consol
idated Mining Company the old board of direc
tors was re-elected, with William Fries as
president and 11. P. Bush secretary.
At the annual meeting; of the Exchequer Min
ing Company the old board of directors was re
elected, with Charles Illrsehfeld as president,
C. E. Elliott fcecretary and A. C. Hamilton su
perintendent.
The Orr Water Ditch Company of Washoe
County. New, has levied an assessment of $3
per share, delinquent November 15.
The directors of the Alta Mining Company
yesterday levied an assessment of 5 cents per
share, delinquent November 22. The directors
also voted a pro rata contribution of 1 per
cent to the fund of the Comstock Pumping As
sociation, and It was partly for this purpose
that the assessment was levied.
The Silver King assessment of 25 cents per
share will be delinquent in the company's office
to-dny.
The Reward Gold Mining Company of Ama
dor County has levied an assessment of 2 cents
per share, delinquent November 21.
The Assockitofl (Juki Mining Company of Col
orado paid a dividend of 1 cent per share, or
$12,500, on the 10th.
The Vindicator mine of Colorado paid a divi
dend of 5 cents per share, or $75,000, on the 10th.
The Bullion-Beck Mining Company of Utah
paid a dividend oi." 10 cents per share, amount-
Ing to $10,<Xi0, on October 10.'; This makes a to
tal of $2,535,000 in dividends since the organi
zation, of which $70,000 has been declared this
year.
At a special meeting of the stockholders of
the Scorpion Company yesterday a reduction
of the capital stock from a par value of $100
to $1 per share was authorized. The directors
also voted a pro rata contribution of 1 per cent
to the fund of the Comstock Pumping Associa
tion.
The Mountain Copper Company of California
paid a dividend of 27Vi cents per share on the
15th. The mine Is in Shasta County, and in six
months of the year 103,000 tons of oru were ex
tracted, averaglifcj 7.53 per cent of copper. The
stacks in process of calcination and local treat
ment at Keswlck were increased, as only 72,000
tons of ore were smelted, producing 4329 tons of
copper in matte, which has been sont to the
New Jersey works to be refined. The delivery
of fine copper from these works during the pe
riod was 3'JSO tons, of which 2800 tons were
marketed, the balance being still in process of
treatment for the separation of its gold and
silver.

The superintendents' reports submitted at the
annual meeting of the Con. Cal. & Va. yester
day show that the yield of the mine for the
past year amounted to 749 tons and 420 pounds
of ore, all of which was milled by the Com
etock Mill and Mining Company at the Mor
gan mill and yielded bullion of the gross as
say value of $15,696 30, showing an average
yield of $20 95 per ton. This bullion contained
$8366 S7 in gold and $7329 42 in silver. The coin
value of the bullion amounted to $11,495 82.
There are 162 tons of ore in the surface bins
of the assay value of $39 97 per ton per mine
car samples; also ESB tons and 150 pounds of
low grade ore, deposited on the ground near
tho orehouse, averaging $10 03 per ton per mine
car samples. "Work in the mine was bus
pended July 15. Superintendent Lyman in his
report recommends further prospecting on the
1750-foot level. In his report Superintendent
Ross states that he has devoted the greater
part of his time to collecting data for use
in the plans for draining the lower levels of
the Comstock lode. Part of this data has
been sent to manufacturers of pumping and
electrical machinery to enable them to bid
for the unwaterir.g of the Comstock flooded
levels. Since Superintendent Ross took
charge no work has been done underground in
the mine. On the surface the work has
been confined to carine for the property of
the company, which is In good condition.
Nothing of any value was found in the
ground worked by the company on the Bruns
wick lode during the past year.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographlc
Office, located In the Merchants' Exchange, Is
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
office, where complete seta of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street, is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Signal
Observatory. Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any, is published
In the morning papers the following day.
CHAS. P. WELCH,
Ensign (retired), U. 8. N., in charge.

NOTE â In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
clay In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column (fives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
an.i then the number piven is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. Th<- plane of
reference is tn â¢ mean of the lower low waters.

Will Draft a New

OAKLAND, Oct. 17.â The ideas of the

This action was a surprise, as it was ex

different tactics.

said to be that the bids for dredging Lake
Merritt, which were opened this morn
ing show that the work can be done for

GRANNAN COMING THIS WAY

Made Quite a

The New Orleans racing game has al
ways had a raft of pluggers in the East,
and when California was mentioned they
always got the hammers ready for action.
From what .the advance guard of ISastorn !
and Middle Western horsemen now on the ;
ground say, the yellow fever scarÂ« Is apt
to cause thl icicles to form on the eaves
of the grand stand at the old Metalrio
course. a prominent layer of prices s-aid

quented by. horse people, that Just before i
leaving Chicago for the coast he heard
a number of bookmakers and owners, who
originally Intended going to New Orleans, j
Kay they would ship to California instead.
This may be. bad news fpr Brother Bush,
but we are inclined to think they will
never regret having changed their minds.

wielder, accompanied by his family, ar
rived in the city yesterday. Mr. Cald
well handled the flag at the recent Los
Angeles meeting, and .while some of his
starts were not up to the average he will
probably be .in form for the Ingleslde

ESCAPED FROM URIAH.
Adolph Hertig Walks to Oakland
and Demands Money From
His Guardian.
OAKLAND, Oct. 17.â Adolph Hertig,
who escaped from the fkiah Insane Asy
lum on the nth inst., appeared before his
guardian, John Nufer, in East Oakland
to-day and demanded money with which
to purchase a new suit of clothes. Ho
had made the trip on foot and had noth
ing to eat or drink en routo. He was
committed to Ukiah by Judge Ellsworth
on September 2t> because of a melan
cholia, and appeared completely exhaust
ed to-day.
Nufer notified Sheriff White and his
ward was this afternoon taken to the
District Attorney's office, where matters
were arranged so that he might continue
to enjoy liberty for a week. And, if
during this period he behaves and acts
rationally ho will probably be restored to
competency. He is possessed of a small
estate, but had heretofore shown a dis
putation to squander all money that came
into his hands in a reckless manner.

A Lovely Life Whose Termination
Will Be Widely Mourned.
OAKLAND, Oct. IT.â The numerous
friends in Oakland and Los Angeles of
Mlsa Birdie Paulsen of Lewiston, this
State, wore greatly shocked to learn of
her sudden death, at the latter place, on
Saturday last. Miss Paulsen, although a
i resident of Trinity County, spent the
[ greater part of her time here and was
' well known in social circles of Oaklandand
