THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, OCTOBER 18, 1893.
TWENTY ACRES
OFFERED FOR
THE CITY'S USE
Lake Improvers Ask
for Its Acceptance.
DREDGING BIDS OPENED
CONTRACTORS WANT ALL THE
WORK DONE AT ONCE.
All the Bids Are Taken Under Ad
visement Pending the Fix
ing of the Tax
Levy.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Oct. 17.
The bids for dredging Lake Merritt were
opened by the Board of Public Works this
morning and several contractors submit
ted their figures.
The meeting was attended by many in
fluential members of the Lake Merritt
Sanitary Improvement Club, Peralta
Heights Improvement Club, Senator
Stratton and others who were present to
offer to the Board of Works in connection
with the dredging: twenty acres of land
which can be used for a park after it is
filled in with the mud from the lake.
The twenty acres of marsh land be
tween Eighth and Twelfth streets were
formally offered to the city through the
Board of Works, and when it is filled in
it is said that the property will be worth
not less than $40,000, for which the city
will have paid nothing.
Senator Stratton tendered the land to
the Board, and in doing so said: "The
Peralta Heights Improvement Club offers
a donation which, unless accepted at this
time, may never be offered again. This
land, at present a nuisance, can be made
a beauty spot, with a fine waterway tor
the passage of boats and with ample pro
visions for flooding the lake. It will make
it possible to remove the car tracks from
Eleventh to Twelfth streets and give a
fine driveway, not only over Eleventh
street, but on a hundred and fifty foot
highway on Twelfth street. I think it is
time that these improvements were madu
and we ask the Board to accept tuo
dredging bid, which provides thy* the city
shall furnish its own dumping ground, so
that we can be assured of this park."
Several other members expressed the
same ideas.
The bids were received from San Fran
cisco Bridge Co.. Quimby & Harrelson,
Pacific Coast Dredging and Reclamation
;ind O. P. Johnson. The bids are
figured out in two ways â one for the wotk
to be completed by June 30 of next your,
another for completion within one year
from date of signing contract. The figures
of the contractors show that it would
be more economical to finish thf work
within the fiscal year. ATI the bids were
read and will be referred to th<- com
mittee of the whole. No action, however,
can be taken until the tax levy is fixed.

To Prohibit Raft Building.
President Hugh Craig of the Chamber
of Commerce received a dispatch from
Lyman T. Gage, Secretary of the Treas
ury, yesterday regarding the danger of
rafting logs and timber from the north
ern ports to this city, to the effect that
while the department recognizes the dan
ger to shipping, still there is no direct
law by which it can be stopped. The only
law that could be construed to serve the
purpose is the act of September 19. 1886;
which states that the creation of any ob
struction not affirmatively authorized by
law to the navigable capacity of any wa
ters in respect to which the United States
has Jurisdiction Is prohibited. It is
probable that the chamber's officers will
take action r>n the lines suggested to pre
vent any more rafts being built.
Illness of Ford Thomas.
The well-known customs broker, W.
Ford Thomas, is seriously ill at his resi
dence from a surgical operation to remove
an abscess In the head. He has a splen
did constitution and though well advanced
in years it Is expected that he will re
cover.
is a tonsorial artist by occupation, wrote
his wife that she should quit writing to
him "You and I can no longer live to
gether till you learn some sense. I don t
think you can get along with anybody,
and there is no use of spoiling two lives.
In another epistle he sent the wife a
few dollars and advised her "to go to
work and learn to be a lady," and that
he was a fool for ever marrying her.
"You are a bad woman at heart, and I
sometimes fear you will poison me. I
did not force you to go away, but now
that you are gone, how pleasant it is.
Go to work and don't live on people. I
never want to see your face again. If
you want to be a sport, all right. I am
turned against you. '

OAKLAND, Oct. 17.â The contest of

to give notice of the petition. The con
testants were given further time to prop
erly am<-n<i their pleadings, and the mat
ter went over for two weeks. The es
tate is valued at about $200,000, of which
nearly $175,000 consists of cash.

HAD TO CHOP
DOWN A DOOR
TO ARREST HIM
Heinous Crime of a
Bootblack.
ASSAULTED A LITTLE GIRL
CAUGHT BY A CITIZEN AFTER
A LIVELY CHASE.
The Father of the Child Wanted to
Shoot the Brute but Was
Dissuaded by His
Friends.
Louis Vanochi, a bootblack, was arrest
ed last night by Officer Pyle and charged
with a heinous offense. His victim is
Ethrl McCarthy, aged 6 years, who lives
at 821 Larkln street.
Shortly after 4 o'clock yesterday after
noon Mrs. McCarthy sent her caughter
on an errand. When passing Vanochi's
stand at Ellis and Larkin streets ho
seized her, so It is claimed, and dragged
her Into a vacant store close by. Becom
ing alarmed over her absence Mrs. Mc-
Carthy started a search for her child,
but failing to find her, she notified Offi
cer Pyle.
While the latter was hunting for the
girl her father, who also had been ap
prised of her disappearance, suspecting
the bootblack, went to the stand. He
was not there, but a boy volunteered the
information that he was in the vacant
store. McCarthy quickly entered the
store and heard his little daughter crying
in a rear room.
The door of the room was securely
locked and McCarthy ordered Vanochi
to open it. IK- at first refused, but as the
indignant father threatened to shoot him
he pulled back the bolt which held the
door and then jumped through an open
transom into tlv yard adjoining. On
the floor lay the unfortunate child, ncr
little face bathed in tears. After giving
her in charge of a citizen who entered the
store at the time, the Indignant father
started after her assailant.
Finding that he was being pursued the
W. C. T. U. IS
WELCOMED
TO BERKELEY
Greeted by Leaders of
the Town.
CONFERENCE WORK BEGINS
DELEGATES HOLD A PUBLIC
MEETING.
Opening of the Nineteenth. Annual
Convention of the California
Woman's Christian Tem
perance Union.
BERKELEY, Oct. 17.â The nineteenth
annual convention of the Woman's
Christian Temperance Union of California
was opened to-night in tne First Pres
byterian Church on Allston way. The
occasion was a public welcome in behalf
of the university town to the 150 and
more delegates who have come from dif
ferent parts of the State to listen to the
annual reports and to talk over the pros
pects for another year's work in the
movement of temperance reform. ;: ; vV r r ; :
The church had been decorated appro
priately by the ladies of the local union.
Banners from the different societies were
hung about the building, showing the del
egates to have come from all sections of
the State, where the white ribbon car
ries its silent crusade. On the platform
sat Mrs. B. Sturtevant-Peet, the president
of the conference; Airs. George W.
Haight, Professor Elmer E. Brown, Rev.
George B. Hatch, William H. Waste, Mrs.
Loulso M. Thomson, Mrs. H. Pierce and
Mrs. E. G. Greene.
After preliminaries of song and prayer
the chairman of the meeting introduced
W. H. Waste, who spoke in behalf of the
city. He welcomed the delegates on be
half of the Board of Trustees. His ad
dress was a glowing tribute to woman's
work in the cause of reform. He took
occasion to say that the charter amend
ment voted upon at the polls in Berkeley
last Saturday was defeated at the in
stance of the saloon keepers.
The Rev. George B. Hatch spoke on be
half of the churches. "There are causes,"
he said, "which make the Individual de
struction of the saloon an impossibility.
One of these is civic opinion, another tho
civic conscience. It is not true that the
majority of our population is against the
saloon. The vast majority favor it, or are
indifferent. The fact is that the popular
conscience takes no heed of it. There is
only one preordained thing to do to change
this condition, and that is the reform of
public opinion and public conscience. The
saloon will stay Just while civic opinion
and civic conscience permit it. If ! the
civic conscience held the saloon as it
held slavery, that evil could not be kept
here even at the cannon's mouth. It is
the great work of reforming the Dublic
conscience that the Woman's Christian
Temperance Union is trying to accom
plish."
In "behalf of the University of Califor
nia Professor Elmer E. Brown welcomed
the delegates to the State University. He
was followed by Mrs. George W. Haight
of the local union.
In response. Mrs. E. G. Greene, the first
vice president of the California Unlon.sev
enteen years ago, spoke on behalf of the
State. She thanked the city and its rep
resentatives for the cordial welcome they
had accorded the temperance workers.
She spoke of the Woman's Christian
Temperance Union as the right hand of
the church, covering over forty depart
ments of work in which the church itself
is engaged. â,,,.â¢
To-morrow morning at 9 o clock the reg
ular session of the conference will begin.

ranged: /. "/"jV

Committees. Officers' reports: Recording
secretary. Mrs. R. A. Emmons; cor

ditor. Mrs. Blanche English; treasurer. Mrs.

and sailors' work. Miss 8. M N. Cummtngs;

Nellie B. Eypter; W. C. T. U. chorus: fran

INDICTMENT DETERMINED ON.
The Grand Jury Did Not Have a
Quorum.
The Grand Jury failed to secure a quo
rum last night for the transaction of
business. Only nine members responded
to the call for the meeting, four of the
others being out of town, one ill and the
others engrossed In politics. It Is under
stood that the jury has determined on
one Indictment in connection with the
School Board lumber shortage. and that
Contractor Swift will play an Important
part in it. The Grand Jury has still in
hand questions relating to the School
Board, and as already intimated in The
Call, will look into the recent appoint
ment of extra teachers. Several witness* I
were subpenaed for this purpose last
evening and will be on hand again when
the jury meets next Thursday.
The Committee on Supervisors is busily
at work looking into certain transactions
of that board and will have a report to
present within a few days.

Berkeley News Notes.
BERKELEY, Oct. 17.-President Martin
J. Kellogg of th.c University of California
has received a communication from the
Italian government asking for a delegate
from the Berkeley faculty to the Twelfth
International Congress of Orientalists, to
be held in Rome. October 2, lSiÂ»9. The
president has appointed Professors J.
Fryer, M. Margolis and H. Nultlng to con
sider the advisability of complying with
the request.
Charles Lees, a boy fourteen years of
age, had his leg broken while Jumping
from the Berkeley local train near Asii
by avenue Etation. Dr. Woodsum Allen,
who was summoned to the case, found a
severe fracture just above the right an
kle.
The West Berkeley branch of the Red
Cross Society forwarded a large box of
books, bandages and other necessaries to
San Francisco to-day. The box will be
put on board one of the transports, soon
to sail to Manila, and used for the sol
diers already at the front.
Wicker's Condition.
ALAMEDA, Oct. 17.â Fred W. Wicker,
â¢who shot himself accidentally yesterday
while cleaning a revolver, passed a meas
urably comfortable day to-clay" and seems
to-night to he holding his own. It is too
early, the doctors in attendance say, to
predict his possible recovery, as a very
slight turn may prove fatal at almost
any time.

CHINESE IN THE WARS.
Two Celestials Complain of Hard
Usag-e at the Hands of Caucasians.
Gow Sin and Qoong Lee Lung, two be
reaved and muchly battered importations
from the land of rice fields and peacock
feathers, appeared before Captain Spil
lane of the Southern Station last evening
and toW a sad story of violent usage at
the hands of two white men earlier in tne
day. Their complaint resulted in the ar
rest of Joseph Wilson and George Bu
chanan, who were charged with battery.
The latter had an additional charge placed
againsa him for carrying a concealed
n, an ugly revolver being found on
his person.
The battle between China and America
was brought about through a misunder
standing of the law of partnership. Fong
Lee and another member of the yellow
persuasion a year ago opened a Junk shop
,'ii 1005 Sixth street. Business prospered,
and the silent partner deciHed to visit the
land of his birth. Not having too high a
regard for Fong Lee's honesty, he em
ployed Gow Sin and Quong Lee Lung to
count bottles and cans and keep a gen
eral lookout for his interests. Fong Lee
was not consulted in the matter, and very
properly became insulted. His Celestial
brain began to concoct plans for his part
ner's representative's removal.
No amount of scheming or bad treat
ment would disconcert Lung and Sin and
at last Fong Lee resorted to harsher
measures. He hired Wilson and Buch
anan, two burly men. to do what he had
failed in and they took the job with alac
rity. The very first day they had a mis
understanding with the Chinamen, which
resulted in a broken nose for Sin and a
nasty scalp wound for Lung and nume
rous bodily aches and pains.
Captain Spillane was appealed to to
mete out Justice, and he promptly de
tailed Detectives Ryan and ODea on the
case, with the result that the two bel
ligerent white men occupy cells and con
dole each other.
What will be done to Fong Lee by his

HE WANTS TO STARVE.
John Galvin in the Receiving Hos
pital Refuses Food.
John Galvin of 553 Natoma street is
locked up in a padded cell in the Receiv
ing Hospital pending an examination as
to his sanity.
Last Sunday Galvin visited St. Rose
Church on Urannan street, and taking up
a position near the chancel rail proceeded
to pray. He had his hat on, and when re
quested to remove it, he refused. Sus
pecting that the man was mentally de
ranged, one of the priests sent for Offi
cer Cooney, who took Gaivln into cus
tody. Since being confined Galvin has re
fused to partake of food. He imagines
that an attempt is being made to poison
him, and rather than allow his enemies
to "do him up," as he expresses it, he is
determined he starve himself. Last night
Superintendent Fisthaler attempted to
force him to take some nourishment, but
failed. He will be sent to Napa to-day.

Korea. All of the above are registered at

EDUCATE THE CUBANS.
If this country wants to blops Cuba, let
it provide free schools in every part of
the island where children live, and also
require that all these children be taught
the English language. That is the only
language on earth in which the principles
of freedom and the practice and philoso
phy of good government can be properly
studied r>y the rising generation. These
great principles are not taught in Span
ish.
It would also be an indescribable bless
ing to the Cubans If it were provided that
after some date in the near future all the
proceedings of the Cuban Legislature
should be conducted in the English lan- i
guage. as well as the proceedings of ci:v ,
and county governments. Also that all
public records be made in duplicate in
English and Spanish: after a while in
English only. One of the greatest bless- i
ings that could possibly be conferred on
the Cubans would be a constitutional pro
vision that twenty years hence only j
those could vote who read English. That j
would be a strong Incentive to youthful i
ambition, and by that time all or nearly j
all of the population would be able to read |
and speak English.
In the meantime let there be no haste !
to haul down the Stars and Stripes In
Cuba, again made sacrr-d by the blood of
our heroes. Cuba should not be consigned
to the doom of numerous South American ;
and Central American countries, in which j
they ha\ - e a republic in name, but a tyr
anny 1 in fact. Let the Cubans rule, but j
they should be made to rule according to !
American notions of civil liberty and good |
government.â Rockvllle Centre Observer, j

SERVANTS IN SANTIAGO.
/
Our servants match the kitchen'to a T.
They are elderly negresses, with families [
of their own, and, like mother birds, they |
nightly cÂ«nvey to the home nest every j
morsel of food not carefully locked up in j
the wardrobe. Their every-day costume i
is distinctive, if not appropriate. . It con- j
sists of a single voluminous white skirt,
very short In front and trailing far be
hind, with a low-necked bodice and short, j
guffed sleeves, leaving the "skinny arms j
are to the shoulders. The front of the
corsage is elaborately embroidered and
secured by a string at the top, tied so
loosely, If tied at all. that a strip of bare
bronze back stands confessed to the waist
line. The woolly heads, gray with the j
weight of years, are topped with gaudy j
turbans; the bare feet are thrust into j
slippers of white canvas, and when my '
lady walks abroad she covers her gaping j
back with a bedraggled white silk shawl. j
Both women consider themselves monu- |
ments of virtuous industry in consenting I
to lend a helping hand to las Americanas,
for could they not, like all their neighbors,
be well fed without work so long as Cu
ban relief supplies hold out? But they
are not injuring their constitutions by
hard labor. When not sitting in the front
window smoking cigarettes and gossiping
with friends outside, their aimless slip
shod feet go slapping about the marble
floors, like the stars, "unhasting yet un
resting." The slow, monotonous slap,
slap, slap of those heelless slippers so
wears upon the nerves that one Indulges
in strange flights of fancy as to what
might accelerate their movements.
Should the seven angels of the Apoca
lypse, carrying the seven golden vials
filled with wrath, heralded by trumpet
notes and wrapped about with awful
glory, come knocking some fine day at
our front door, slap, slap, would go those
same Blow feet to admit them.
SHOWED OFF TO DISADVANTAGE
A young organist living In Chicago was
fortunate in securing permission to prac
tice on the big organ in the Auditorium,
says the Record, of that city. One morn-
Ing he sat at the higher keyboard, trifling
with the music, improvising and playing
odds and ends. An elderly man walked in
and took a seat a few rows away from
the musician. The young organist noticed
him. and was encouraged to "show off"
and to do a few trloks of playing for his
audience. He rambled on for an hour,
and the elderly man sat there, apparently
impressed.
The young man tired at last, and was
about to lock the organ, when the elderly
man approached him, and said in broken
English that he wished to play for a few
minutes.
"They don't allow any one but an ex
perienced organist to touch the instru
ment," said the young man, loftily. With
a little gesture, suggestive of meekness
and humility, the stranger presented his
card:
\LEXANDRB GUILMANT,
PARIS.
Then It was time for the young organist
to swoon. He had missed the chance of
his life. For an hour he had been enter
taining the great master with' home-made
drivel.

COMPLAIN ONLY
OF THE CLIMATE
Hospital Patients Visited

SICK MEN ARE CONTENT
COMMISSIONERS FIND ALL SE
RENE IN JACKSONVILLE.
General Dodge Congratulates Sur
geons on Their Excellent Man
agement of the Hos
pitals.
I Special EHspatch to ThÂ© Call.
ACKSONVILLE. Fla., Oct. 17.â The
Lr Investigating: Commission spent
afternoon inspecting the camps in
immediate vicinity of Jacksonville,
leavy rain was falling during a part
the day, but the commissioners per
formed their self-appointed task of go-
Ing through the camp from one end to
the other in a very painstaking man
ner.
The commission interviewed privates
and officers alike, and made personal
K:s to the quarters of the men and
r commanders. They lifted the
â¢rs o-f the commissary stoves, went
ugh the kitchens, went through the
ilrug stores, inspected the apparatus,
talked with physicians and nurses and
made special Inquiry into the method
jf conducting the hospitals. All three
of the division hospitals were visited
and inspected, the commissioners go-
Ing through the tents, and in some in
stances where conversation was al
lowable, talking with the patients.
They found the patients to be ap
parently well cared for, and so far as
could be discovered, they were as con-
Rd as sick men generally are. Many
em were reading books and maga
zines, but in a great number of cases
the men clasped closely in their arms
letters which had evidently been re
: Hum borne. The patients were
ail found occupying clean beds or
:ots, and in no instance was a hospital
tent or pavilion found to be without a
Boor. In most cases the hospital
Lents were effectually screened
igainst Hies and mosquitoes.
But one complaint was heard in the
entire round, and this was evidently
not made in a spirit of fault-nnding.
Colonel Junes of South Carolina found
fault with the climate, saying that
fully 20 per cent of his men were ill,
largely on this account. He also said
rations of refrigerator beef had
been received in bad condition, which
Kras due to- the weather. Summing up
the result of the inspection, General
Dodge, the chairman, said:
"We have compared notes after the
day's work and find that complaint was
made from but one regiment during the
day. This was the objection made to
the climate by Colonel Jones. We
saw both officers and men, sick and
well, and we gave all an opportunity to
be heard, asking them in the absence
of their officers, if they had any criti
cism to make.
"We shall to-morrow begin the in
quiry by swearing the men under oath
and we hope, if there are any short
comings, to thus uncover them. We
find the sick doing exceptionally well,
and we are pleased to congratulate
their physicians on the excellent ar
rangements made for their care."
This statement was made in the
presence of the members of the com
mission and was indorsed by them.
A HARROWING EXPERIENCE.
Forty-four years ago, when Admiral
Henry Erben, retired, was a young Lieu
tenant in the United States Navy, he had
an experience wmch for horror equals
any yarn ever spun by sailor. For sev
enty "days he and a prize crew suffered
as "few " men havo suffered. He was
aboard the bark Amelia, a lllibuster,
taken at Port au Prince, Hayti, and sent
to New York by the American Consul.
Rats were used as food for a consider
able time in that voyage, according to
the story of the veteran officer.
Admiral Erben says that the Amelia
was litted out in 1854 as a bark, loaded
v.ith rifles and munitions of war and sent
to the Florida banks to meet a hteamer.
She was a mere hulk and it was supposed
she would be sunk as soon as her cargo
Transferred. But the Government,
having notice of the expedition, detained
the steamer and the bark put into Port
au Prince, where she was abandoned.
She reached the port late in 1854, every
body but the cook leaving her. Erben
was on duty with the Saratoga, then in
port there.
In November the American Consul seiz
ed the Amelia and it was decided to send
her north with a prize crew. Lieuten
ants Reed, Werden, Maxwell and Erben,
and a crew of eleven men were put in
charge, rations taken aboard for thirty
days and the vessel put to sea. Admiral
says they had scarcely got out of i
port when the water-logged condition of
the prize was noticed. But the vessel
was held to her course, the men going to
the pumps on the first day oyt.
Hatteras Inlet was reached in Decem
ber. There the vessel encountered a ter
rilic gale and for days was a prey to the
waves. All the spars went over and
nobody aboard expected to survive the
journey. But the bark weathered the
gale and an effort was made to run In
shore and beach her. This failed, and
Mr. Were' en decided to run for the Ber
mudas. Unfavorable winds and the crip
plied condition of the boat made this a
failure and the course was set for St.
Thomas. Danish West Indies. Meanwhile
nearly all the provisions had been con
sumed and the survivors of the crew were
reduced to thi necessity of eating the
rats which infest all old vessels.
When they had been out forty or fifty
d< ya the rats began to fail. Mr. Erben
noticed that the animals seemed well fed,
BO he decided there was food in the hold.
He Investigated and found a couple of
barrels of rice and some molasses. Then
they overhauled v British vessel, received
some f.our and rum. and finally reached
Bt Thomas after soventy days on the
sea. They all suffered from scurvy be
; cause of the lack of suitable food, and
I many of the men lost their lives in the
gales they encountered.
Ninety days after they set sail the sur
vivors "reached New York. The Secre
tary of the Navy had been much worried
over them and ships were out scouting
the sea for some trace of them. When
they reached America they had the pecu
liar experience of reading their own
obitunric-s in the papers. The useless old
bark was sold for $105, for the vaiue of a
new mainyard she carried, was towed to
sea and sunk.
Admiral Erben Is now In charge of the
fleet having for Its work the defense of
the Atlantic coast. He is an old and
tried naval officer, with a record as good
as the best. He has sf en active service
in many seas, and is probably taking part
in his last war.â Chicago Chronicle!
HOW THE SCALLOP TRAVELS.
Many people are very fond of scallops,
as may be judged from the fact that
nearly 00,000 gallons of the meats were
used in New York and Brooklyn in 1897.
Of course these bivalves likewise are
hunted persistently, and the prospect is
that before very long comparatively few
of them will be left. It has been sug
gested that they might be propagated in
great salt ponds, which could be util
ized as preserves. Unquestionably they
could be bred by mixing the milk of the
male Bballops with the eggs of the fe
males, hatching them out and liberating
the: young ones. Several difficulties are
In the way, however, and the most im
portant of these ts the locomotive habit
of the scallop. It is by no means a sed
entary animal, like the oyster or long
clam, but, on the contrary, is addicted to
swimming about, more like a fish than
like a respectable mollusk. Not many
years ago a Frenchman bought several
thousand scallops and planted them in
beds. The next day he was very much
astonished to tind that they had all taken
French leave.
The scallop accomplishes locomotion by
a series of leaps. When it is alarmed,
or wishes to change its location, it opens
and energetically closes its valves, thus
expelling the water; the reaction shoots
it backward. By this means the creature
is able to travel iong distances. Some
times scallops make considerable Jour
neys in large companies. One can scarce
ly imagine a lovelier sight than that of a
flock of these pretty creatures, with
shells of every hue, from purple and
white to black, enlivened with shades of
pink, yellow and fawn, darting about in
clear water. In their flightlike move
ments, vertical, horizontal and zigzag,
they are more suggestive of a flock of
winged animals than of bivalve mollusks.
A superb species called Pecten Islandicus,
formerly abundant on the coast of Maine,
is now so nearly extinct that a specimen
is regarded as a prize by conchologists.
Long Island Sound, New York Harbor,
and the New Jersey coast have been
fairly depopulated of scallops by raking
and dredging.â Boston Transcript.
BICYCLISTS AND WILD ANIMALS
Are our British birds beginning, like
the British public, to appreciate the
bicycle? I am inclined to think they
are, writes Dr. Charles Plowrlght in
Natural Notes. Certainly one catches
many more glimpses of bird life when
on the wheel than the uninitiated
would expect. It seems as if both
birds and wild animals have not the
same fear of a cyclist that they have
of an ordinary walking human being.
The cyclist's approach is practically
noiseless, and his image upon their re
tinae purely momentary, besides which
he never carries a gun nor a catapult,
and is unaccompanied by a dog. It is
by no means impossible, therefore, that
these creatures should associate him
with their less dangerous enemies. It
is pretty generally accepted that rooks
j know when a man is carrying a gun,
I and it seems by no means impossible
that birds should associate the peculiar
mode of progression of the cyclist with
safety. Be this as it may, they let you
get much nearer them when you are
cycling than when you are walking.
The other evening we passed within a
yard or so of a pair of partridges, who
were squatting on the grass by the
roadside, without disturbing them.
True they had the "corner of their
eyes" upon us, but they never stirred.
Then, again, chaffinches, when they are
feeding on the road, fly up and alight
on the nearest branch without troub
ling to go any considerable distance, as
j they do when one is walking. One is
j apt, however, to get somewhat different
j idea as to the relative frequency of
! birds in a district from the saddle of a
j cycle t h.;ui one would from walking.
For instance, the other evening we had
a run of some fifteen miles in haste to
avoid a threatened storm, through
country lanes with high thorn hedges,
interspersed with a fair number of trees
and thickets. The evening was chilly,
but the nightingales were in full song,
and we seemed hardly to have got be
yond the range of the song of one be
fore we came into that of another. The
; natural impression one would axrive at
| was that the country was full of nlght
i ingales; possibly this impression may
not be so far wrong, for there seem to
j be more nightingales this year than
| there were swallows; but It must be
; remembered that people do not walk
fifteen miles in an evening as a rule,
certainly they do not cover the dis
tance in an hour and a half. The
cycle, too, is so noiseless that the bird
never hears you and his song Is not in
terrupted.
THE KOREAN KING'S SON.
A rather Interesting bit of gossip about
a foreign potentate In which a former
Franklin County (Ohio) boy. Dr. Newton
Allen, figures in an Asiatic, court. Is being:
told of the Crown Prince of the little
kingdom of Korea.
After a not wholly pleasant snjourn in,
America tho Crown Prince of Korea has
squared accounts with his landlady, paid
all of his outside bills, discharged his two
attendants, bid the Presbyterian Board of
Foreign Missions good-by and sailed for
Japan and home. The Prince came here
a year ago â Just why no one seems to
know. At first It appeared he came to oe
educated, but if that was the errand It
Beenu to have been poorly carried out.
Thorp were rumors that reasons of state
In Korea dictated his absence for a time.
At any rate, he was sent out by his
father, the King, the money for his sup
port passing through the hands of the
senior secretary of the Presbyterian
l">nrd. He first went to I\ew York and
then to Washington, where his stay waa
kept at secret as possible.
only once did ha come into notice, and
that was when he had a quarrel with his
landlady. He was hard up most of the
time, remittances not coming regularly
from home. At first he studied furiously,
mastering English fairly well in a short
time. Then, all of a sudden, he would
not study at all, but lived in complete idle
ness. Now, when he departs, he does so
hurriedly and without consulting any one.
He is the heir Oi the Korean throne and
the son of the Queen who was murdered,
as the public will remember. It was an
incident on the night of the murder that
brought the King into friendly relations
with the Presbyterian board, with Dr.
Newton Allen, formerly of Central Col
lege, as the hero.
There was an uprising of tho populace
in the capital, and the royal family was
in grave danger, and indeed the Queen
was murdered by the mob, when Dr. Al
len, who was In the city as the represen
tative of the Presbyterian Board of For
eign Missions, stepped in and, at the risk
of his life, succeeded in quelling the riot
and saving the King and hie family. Since
that fateful hour Newton Allen, the far
mer boy from Central College, has been
a man of more than passing popularity.
Mr. Allen was the secretary of the
Korean legation to this country during
the greater jyirt of Cleveland's first ad
ministration and through President Har
rison's entire administration.
The Crown Prince will possibly return
to Korea now, whore he will be under tae
watchful eye of his American counselor. â

UPON A MULTITUDE OF CONFLICTING OPINIONS
MRS. BOTKIN BASES HER FIRST FIGHT FOR FREEDOM
Snperior Court Judges in Bank
Listen to a War of
References.
Judge Carroll Cook, with his Judicial
confreres. Judges Borden, "Wallace,
Trout t and Seawell, listened yesterday to
the arguments of counsel and prosecution
on the petition of the defense for the
freedom of Mrs. Cordelia Botkin, alleged
poisoner, by habeas corpus.
It was the first day of serious business
that has been permitted since the spec
tacular prosecution of Mrs. Botkin was
begun. The horseplay, the opera bouffe
effects and the lack-wit Interruptions that
have characterized the case on each occa
sion that it has had hearing were for the
day eliminated.
It was a battle without bitterness and
with sharp argument instead of innuendo
as the sole weapon.
There were no new points developed.
The two original contentions that Knight
and Hosmer have held to since extradi

a Governor's Decision.

Chief of the Chinese Bureau to Be
Assigned to Other Work.
As a result of the scandalously ineffi
cient and. careless manner In which the
work of the Chinese Bureau has been
conducted the Secretary of the Treasury
notified Collector Jackson yesterday that
he had sent three men from .Washington
to take charge of the bureau. Messrs.
Meredith, Flesh and Urquhart will be as
slgT.ed to the customs fervico, and the
many scandals which have dieFÂ«-aced the
management of the bureau will probably
be at an end.
There is no reflection on the manner In
â which Inspectors Flesh and Urquhart
have performed their duties, but they
â will have to go in order to make room
for the two Inspectors who accompany
the new chief from Washington.
The news has filled the highbinder ele
ment with consternation.

Mr "knight avoided saying where the

and shows no telling signs of her long
confinement unless, perhaps, by an in
creased nervousness.

with the public, was shocked at the dis
closures of the revolting practice.
The uses to which the human skin is
put after It is cut from the bodies of the
unclaimed dead, at the Slate arid city
public institutions, have been further In
vestigated. That the skin is unlawfully
used every physician seen admits. It has
been asked, however, to what extent this
use has been carried.
A physician of the beet standing, who
asked to have his name withheld, said on
this point:
"I have a fine writing desk, which I
consider a work of art. A iailor was
brought to the City and County Hospital.
On his chest was, tattooed in red and
black inks, two large American eagles. I
examined the man's heart, and so saw
the tattooing, which was beautifully done.
Well, the man died, and his skin was no
longer of any use to him. He was buried
all right and I em sure never missed his
eagles. They make about as handrome
a writing desk as I have eVer seen. I
went through Cooper, and while all the
bodies were not skinned by any means,
I should say about one in a hundred went
to make slippers or anything else for the
students.
"Now, an interne at the City and Coun
ty does about what he likes. There is a
tannery at Twenty-sixth and Howard
streets, which tans skins beautifully."
Dr. Barbat of the Physicians' building,
on Suttt:r street, who was formerly dem
onstrator of anatomy at Toland, made
little secret of the student uses for skin.
He said:
"Of course It is against the rules. It Is
stealing; but what sre you going to do
with any one who steals? This using the
nkln is a more mutter of sentiment, noth
ing more. No, I s-hould not care to have
my wife's skin cut off or tanned or worn
after she was dead. I know the students
at Toland do steal the skin. I did myself
and know students who have had it made
Into slippers, book covers or anything
else that handsome leather can be made
up into. "Why, I wore a watch guard of
human skin for years. It was strong and
soft and I tanned it myself. I would be
wearing it to-day, only my wife did not
like the idea of having it about and so
gave me this handsome guard on condi
tion that I gave up my oia one. It is all
a matter of sentiment. I was In
charge of the dissecting room, skin was
taken. I could not keep it. I have tried
to stop it certainly, but boys will be boys,
and. they think It smart.

upon one proposition, namely, can this
woman, accused of crime by and in a for
eign State in which she has never been, be
taken from her sovereign State, from her
friends, her home and away from the
witnesses that the constitution demands
that she shall be confronted with, and
tried for her life?
"I say that she cannot, and every ref
erence and every decision that I can find
says 'no.' "
Mr. Knight followed with an exposition
of that portion of the Declaration of In
dependence which provides against per
sons charged with crime being taken over
seas and away from friends Â«nd wit
nesses, for trial. He then brought the
constitution to bear him out in his con
tention that the law does not recognize a
"constructive" fugitive, and also quoted,
as more modern testimony to the same
point, the case of Hall that came up as
an extradition matter between North
Carolina and Tennessee.
"The murderer was imprisoned in
Xorth Carolina, charged with being a fu
gitive from the justice of Tennessee, for
having killed within the latter State one
Andrew Bryson." stated Mr. Knight.
TJie prisoner was tried and convicted of
murder, but the judgment was reversed
by the Supreme Court, which also denied
extradition, concluding its opinion as
follows:
Courts enforce laws, not simply to punish
the gulity but as well to protect the Innocent.
ever, as the subjects are scarce. We have I
trouble with the physicians at the City
and County Hospital. They perform au

"It is pretty leather, and most of the
students have sweethearts, and the jirls
get the leather when they want it."
It seems really a fit subject for a pes
simist when a girl, refined in all her
tastes, as the world goes, will wear the
skin of a human being. She would prob
ably faint away at the idea of a dissect
ing room or an operating room. The
sight of blood or death would overcome
her and send her Into hysterics.
Just such a girl displaying her belt said
unabashed: "I do not see anything so
dreadful about It. At first I did not quite
like it. It certainly is beautiful, and you
can see how soft and strong it is, and
how nicely it fits into the figure."
"But think of the man who owned It."
"Oh, It was not a man."
"How do you know?"
"Why it was a woman, and her name
was Carrie. My friend knew her. There
were some hairs on It at first, but they
wore off."
"Were you ever In a hospital?"
"Yes; I do a great deal of work among
the poor. I would do a great deal for
them."
"Did you ever tell any of them you
were visiting the story of your pretty
belt?"
"Oh, that is a different matter, but they
do not feel things as we who are educat
ed do."
"No?"
"Why everybody knows that. Their
sensibilities are not like ours."
"You go to church, do you not?"
"Yes. certainly. I am not a heathen."
"Does it not "seem a little 'spooky' at
night alone in your room with those
things?"
"To tell you the honest truth, I always
whipped my belt and things into a bureau
drawer at first. I do not mind it a bit,
now. Only people are so odd. I had my
purse in my hand and put It up to ray
mouth. A girl who was with me was hor
rified. I think It was because she has no
human skin things of her own."
"Who gave you yours?" ' _ .
"A student at Cooper. He had the belt
made nt Shreve's, and I think It Is lovely.
It Is half a sotivenlr, too, for he gave me

knew the separation would be hard for us
"You are engaged to him, then?"
"Oh, yes; there's no secret about It."

and lived and suffered and enjoyed and

The law allows and science demands
that bodies of human beings be used for
I study, but there is a very stringent law
ajralnst willfully mutilating or defacing
i the dead. The law requires the burial or
cremation of every portion of the bodies
after dissection except such as are of use
to science, such as the bones. Now that
the attention of right-thinking people is
turned to the real horror of the manufac
ture there can be little doubt that the

The law which fails to provide for the extra
dition of a guilty man must be understood ana
adhered to, because it may be invoKeu as a
protection to the innocent, who are prosfoutpa
without cause against the annoyance, Â«<P"" t Â«;

After having given to their Honors a
list of his further references Mr. Knight
gave way to Assistant District Attorney
Hosmer, -who, for the principal ,argu
ment, took up the case that Mr. Knight
had closed with.
The noon hour shut off the Hosmer ar
ment, but at 2 o'clock he resumed, say
ing:
To the opinion that Mr. Knight cited there are
two dissenting opinion;:.
Justice Clark handed down a dissenting opin
ion, and Justice MÂ«cKa>> concurred. The points
raised in the dissenting opinion are that a par
son who places himself outsida the limits
of the State from thence to commit the crime
within said State, and ever afterward iivolus
going Into said State to avoid arrest, as truly
"fleps from Justice" as he who having com
mitted a crime flees from the State subsequent
ly. If an infernal machine sent by mall >>r ex
press from a distant State explodes nnd kills
the receiver It Is murder committed In the
latter State. The sender skulking in another
Statf- to avoid arrest Is as truly a fugitive from
Justice as if he had accompanied the machine
to Its destination and then tied. The consti
tutional provision for extradition, and the laws
passed In pursuance thereof, it should lie re
membered, are not criminal but remedial pro
visions. They therefore should be liberally con
btrued to effect the purpose Intended to be
served. Civilized man must recoil from the
practical ruling that the territory lUljncrnt to
State boundaries Is "no man's land, ' and that
murder is privileged If committed across a
State line.
A fugitive from Justice Is pimply one who,
having committed a crime within a State keeps
himself beyond the ordinary process of the
courts of such State. Comity certainly should
recognize that murder is a high uff.-nsc against
the laws of a eister State, and we should re
fuse to shelter the offender when demanded for
trial.
Many other technical points were raised,
but seemed to meet with but little ap
proval. At the conclusion of Hosmer s
argument General Chamberlain, special
counsel for Chief Lees, addressed the
court. He adhered to the same line of
argument pursued by Hosmer and had
not concluded when an adjournment was
taken until 10:30 this morning-.

SPEAR AFTER BUTTE.
Has Filed Charges Against the Rev-
enue Cutter Commander.
Navigator Butte of the revenue cutter
Golden Gate has locked horns again with
Surveyor of Customs Spears and a formal
complaint has been filed against the navi
gator by the Surveyor accusing him of
disobeying the orders of his superior offi
cers. Yesterday on the arrival of the
Doric the customs officers. In charge of
Deputy Surveyor Chauncey Milton St.
John, drew alongside the Doric and de
manded to be allowed on board the ves
sel for the purpose of preventing smug
gling. Butte stated that the quarantine
examination was not yet concluded and
that he would not admit the officers. He,
however, agreed to admit certain in
spectors, whom he named, but Deputy
St. John demanded that they all be ad
mitted or none, and that he was willing
that they should share the fate of the
vessel whether the vessel went into quar
antine or not. Butte refused, and kept
the inspectors waiting until Dr. Rosenau
was ready. Hence the trouble.
"RAW WORK."
The Secretary of the Treasury Stops
Competition of Italian Arg-ols.
Customs Collector Jackson received yes
terday a letter from the Secretary of the
Treasury, designed to correct an Incur
sion Into Uncle Sam's revenue strong box.
It is as follows:
Referring to tho reciprocal agreement be
tween this country and France, 1 have to In
form you that the department Is advised that
large quantities of crude argols have been
shipped from Italy to MarÂ«r?!!ip:, for exporta
tion to the United States and have been ad
mitted without difficulty an products of France
at the reduced rate of duty provided In said
agreement.
You will please cause the matter to be. inves
tigated and hereafter require such evidence as
you may deem proper as to the country of pro
duction of all articles claimed to be entitled
to the privileges conferred by said agreement.
Souvenir Day.
As an Instance of what may be accom
plished In commercial life by determined
and enterprising young men, probably no
firm in this city would serve as a better
example than that of Marks Brothers,
who to-day open to the public their en
larged premises at 1212 and 1214 Market
street. In ISBI, at the ageof 12 years, Mr. L.
Marks started business in a small way on
his own account at 106 Sixth street, and
five years later was joined by his brother,
Mr. AY. Marks. To-day these two establish
ments employ seventy-five girls in the fac
tories alone, while thirtv-nve girl clerks
are required in their retail departments.
The firm manufactures ladles', children's
and Infants' wear, and are Importers of
laces, embroideries, hosiery and corsets.
They sell only to the retail traxle at
wholesale prices.
The Messrs Marks are both native sons,
having been born in this city, and the
present magnitude of their enterprises Is
a glowing tribute to their native business
ability. The Market-street store has been
greatly enlarged and admirably equipped
to handle the large volume of trade, which
this firm has justly earned. To each visi
tor to-day will be offered a slight remem
brance or souvenir, between^ the hours of
10 to 12 a. m. and 2 to 5 p. nf â¢

ESCAPED FROM
JAPAN TO LAND
IN JAIL HERE
James Flood Arrested
on Arrival.
ACCUSED OF A GRAVE CRIME
TAKEN FROM THE DORIC BY
FEDERAL OFFICERS,
Formerly a Resident of San Fran
cisco and Only Recently Went
to the Orient to Engage
In Business.
James Flood, formerly of San Francisco,
but more recently of Kobe, Japan, spent
last night In the Alameda County Jail,
and the Indications are that It will be
a lonp time before he breathes the air of
freedom again.
Flood arrived from the Orient on the
steamer Doric yesterday, and was ar
rested before he could make his escape
from the steamer by United States Mar
shal Shine and United States Secret Ser
vice Agent Browne, upon a cablegram
from the United States Consul at Kobe,
stating that Flood was a fugitive from
justico and asking that he be apprehend
ed. Marshal Shine received the cable
gram several days ago and has been en
the watch for the Doric ever since. When
the steamer's arrival was reported by the
Merchants' Exchange, the officers stared
out on a tug, hoping to intercept her
before she reached her dock. In this i.icy
failed, but they managed to get along
side before the passengers disembarked,
and hunting up the purser they soon had
Flood in custody. He was all ready to
leave the ship, axid was exceedingly ner
vous. He did not manifest any surprise,
evidently anticipating that officers would
be watching for him, but hqr>lng to elude
them before they could identify him.
When taken into the purser's room
Flood broke down completely. He was
joined by a friend, who Informed him
that his sister arrived from New York
a few days ago to meet him here. Flood
asked to be allowed to see his sister, but
the officers advised against it until to-day,
In order that he might regain his com
posure.
The prisoner was taken at once to the
Alameda County Jail and Marshal Shine
notified the Attorney General at Wash
ington and the Consul at Kobe of the ar
rest. During the trip across the bay
Flood attempted to tell the officers of his
troubles, but could scarcely utter a dozen
words before his emotions would over
come him and his statement was almost
incoherent.
The officers gleaned, however, that
Flood went to Japan a few months ago
to engage In business with hia twin
brother, who was the must extensive deal
er in carpets and mattings in the Orient,
besides being Identified with a banking
concern. Upon his arrival in Kobe his
brother turned over the business. to him
and departed hastily for the United
States, going to New York, where he
now is. Flood found that his brother had
become involved in financial difficulties to
the extent of between JIOO.oOO and $:200,000.
He would not state the nature of the
trouble, but said he had consulted with
the best legal talent in Japan and it was
on the advice of his attorneys that he de
cided to follow his brother to the United
States. â¢
Flood, who Is about 38 years old, said he
had lived in this city the greater portion
of his life, and that his father died here
several months ago, leaving a valuable es
tate to the three children.
The cablegram received by Marshal
Shine does not state what offense Flood
is accused of and until the officer hears
from Japan, and from. the Attorney.Gen
eral, he cannot say what will: be donewith
the prisoner. From remarks made .by
Flood the Impression prevails that he will
have to face a charge of embezzlement.
OAKLAND, Ocj. James Flood was
brought to the Aiameda County Jail this
evening by Deputy United States Mar
shals Farrlsh and Harry Moffltt. The
prisoner complained of being very tired
and his pale face and general demeanor
bore this out. The greatest latitude con
sistent with the rules governing the safe
ty of prisoners was extended to Flood, and
at 9:30 this evening he enjoyed a substan
tial supper in the reception room. He re
ceived a number of visitors who made
calls of condolence. During the evening
John Livingston was one of the first call
ers. Later Mr. 'Flood's mother in law and
her son hold a long consultation with the
prisoner.
To Jailer Orrin Page the prisoner gave
strict orders that he did not wish to be
bothered by reporters, as he had no state
ment to make. -
"I have nothing whatever to say," said
Mr. Flood to a Call reporter. "When I
am ready to make a statement I will do
so without being' pressed. What I was
arrested for I would like to know my
self, for I really do not know. I did
have an idea but I do not care to ex
press it. I am not a resident of San
Francisco, and I do not wish to say who
will be my attorney in this matter. I
did not come from Kobe under an as
sumed name, I inn't do business that
â¢way. I cannot refer you to anybody for
particulars â  regarding my arrest, be
cause I need no one to advise me and
always attend to my affairs personally."
Flood intimated that the charges were
not of a serious cnaracter and made light
of the subject. He seemed particularly
hopeful that his sister should not learn
of nls arrest to-night. "While the prisoner
observed the strictest secrecy, so far as
giving anything to tho press was con
cerned, he talked to Jailer Page, but even
to him he. denied that he knew why he
was arrested. The cablegram on which
be was arrested simply commanded , the
authorities to hold him on ' a charge of
fraud, and in conversing with Page he re
marked that it did not amount to any
thing. It was evident that Flood did not
propose to make any definite statement
until he had consulted his attorney. He
told the jailer that he had extensive
banking interests in Japan, that he was
also interested in shipping and that flve
of the company's vessels are now en
route from Japan to New York, where he
has an office. He said, too, that he had
left here on the Contlc in regard to the
annexation proposition. * From the trend
of his conversation with the jailer it ap
pears he became dissatisfied with the
business at Kobe, for he remarked that
"he threw up the business and /Vent
away." Hfs wife and family, however,
remained in Japan. ...

NEW PARK COMMISSIONER.
Frederick W. Zeile Installed as Guar-
dian of Golden Gate Park.
Yesterday afternoon Fred W. Ziele was
Installed as a Commissioner of Golden
Gate Park. He succeeds Joseph Austin,
and was appointed by Governor Budd a
few days ago. The installation was very
informal and took place in the Park
Lodge. Mr. Austin merely introduced the
gentleman who is to be one of the park
guardians for the coming four years, and
remaked that he was happy to have the
pleasure of presenting so worthy a suc
cessor.
Mr. Zeile is not yet conversant with the
exact condition of the park, hut rnntcni
plates making extensive Improvements
should the funds permit. He thinks that
the park should be connected with the
Presidio reservation and that the sweep
ings of the streets of this city should be
used in fertilizing the grounds. After the
ceremony at Park Lodge the new Com
missioner, In company with Superintend
ent McLaren, Adolph Spreckels and Jos.
Austin, drove through the park and dis
cussed future improvements.
